Salah — Prayer
beginner
8 min
Four Prohibited Actions in Prayer: Turning Pebbles, Cracking Fingers, Intertwining Fingers, and Resting on Hips
Provides hadith evidence and legal classification for four specific actions: turning pebbles, cracking fingers, intertwining fingers, and resting on hips.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (makruh tahrimi) for each of the four actions, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the Hanafi legal reasoning based on explicit prohibition (nahy) and classification as 'abath.

Core Idea
Four futile actions in prayer are prohibitively disliked due to explicit prophetic prohibitions and consensus.

In the Hanafi school, the prayer is a sacred act requiring focus and humility. The scholars identified specific actions that contradict this spirit and are thus considered makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). This lesson examines four such actions—turning pebbles, cracking fingers, intertwining fingers, and resting on hips—along with their textual proofs from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four actions and their rulings
Read the passage and list each of the four actions mentioned. For each, note the explicit ruling (makruh tahrimi in prayer) and any exceptions (e.g., turning pebbles allowed once for prostration).
2
Extract the primary hadith evidence
For each action, locate the hadith cited as proof. Identify the source (e.g., Bukhari, Muslim, Sunan) and the specific wording of the prohibition.
The text quotes multiple hadith; note the chain or narrator where given.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
Explain how the Hanafi school derives the ruling of karahah tahrimiyyah from these hadith. Apply the usul principle that an explicit prohibition (nahy) indicates tahrim unless there is evidence to lower it. Note also the reasoning of 'abath (futility) and resemblance to disbelievers or people of Hell.
The text mentions 'abath and imitation of the people of Lot for cracking fingers, and the posture of the people of Hell for takhassur.
4
Note any exceptions or variations
Identify the exception for turning pebbles (once for prostration) and the distinction for cracking fingers outside prayer (tanzihi unless for need). Understand why these exceptions exist.
The exception for pebbles is based on the hadith 'if you must, then once'.
5
Summarize the practical takeaway
Consolidate the four rulings into a single principle: any action that distracts or resembles prohibited behavior is makruh tahrimi in prayer. Apply this to similar modern actions (e.g., fiddling with a phone).
The underlying criterion is 'abath and distraction from khushu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقَلْبُ الحصى، إلا للسجودِ، مرة) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « لا تمسح الحصى وأنتَ تُصَلِّي، فإنْ كنتَ لا بد فاعِلاً فواحدةً» (۲) متفق عليه، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « إِذَا قَامَ أَحَدُكُمْ فِي الصَّلاةِ فَلا يَمْسَحُ الْحَصَى فَإِنَّ الرَّحْمَةَ تُوَاجِهُهُ» (۳) رواه أصحاب السنن. وقول جابر بن عبد الله: « سألت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عـن مســح الحصى فقال: واحدة ولأن تمسك عنها خيرٌ لكَ مِنْ مَائَةِ ناقَةٍ سودِ الحدقِ » ) (وفَرْقَعَةُ الأصابع) ولو مرة، وهو: أن يغمزهــا أو تُصَوّت، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ( لا تفرقع أصَابِعَكَ وأنتَ فِي الصَّلاةِ » ) (ولأنــه نوع من العبث، كذا في ( البرهان ). وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « الصَّاحِكُ فِي الصَّلاةِ والملتَفِتُ والمفرقع أصَابِعَهُ سواءٌ ( ) يعني : في الإثم كذا في مجمع الروايات » ، وإنمـا كــره لأنـه عـمـل قـوم لوط، فيكره التشبه بهم، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام لعلي ه: « إني أحـب لـك مـا أحـب لنفسي، لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنْتَ تُصَلِّي ) () كذا في المستصفى » ، وينبغي أن تكون الكراهة تحريمية للنهي الوارد في ذلك، ولأنها من أفراد العبث كذا في ( البحر ) ، والإجماع على كراهتها في الصلاة انتهى. كذا في « الدراية» بخلاف الفرقعة خارج الصلاة لغير حاجة فإنها تنزيهية، (وتشبيكها) لقول ابـن عمر فيه: « تِلْكَ صَلاةُ المغضوب عليهم ) ( ورأى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجلاً قد شبك أصابعه في الصلاة، ففرج عليه الصلاة والسلام بين أصابعه ) والتَّخَصُرُ) لقول أبي هريرة : ( نَهَى رسولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يصلي الرجل متخصراً ) (٨) وروي عنه عليه السلام: أن التخصر راحة أهل النار (٩) انتهى.
And turning the pebbles, except for prostration, once) due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not wipe the pebbles while you are praying, and if you must do so, then only once' (2) agreed upon. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'When one of you stands in prayer, do not wipe the pebbles for mercy faces him' (3) narrated by the authors of Sunan. And the saying of Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'I asked the Prophet about wiping the pebbles, and he said: once, and if you refrain from it, it is better for you than one hundred black-eyed camels.' (And cracking the fingers) even once, and it is: to press them or make a sound, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer' and because it is a type of futility, as in al-Burhan. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'The one who laughs in prayer, the one who looks around, and the one who cracks his fingers are equal' meaning in sin, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. And it is disliked because it is the action of the people of Lot, so imitating them is disliked. He said, peace be upon him to Ali: 'I love for you what I love for myself: do not crack your fingers while you are praying' as in al-Mustasfa. And it is appropriate that the dislike (karahah) is tahrimi (prohibitive) due to the prohibition regarding that, and because it is an instance of 'abath, as in al-Bahr. And consensus on its dislike in prayer. End. As in al-Diraya, unlike cracking outside prayer without need, which is tanzihi (lightly disliked). (And intertwining them) due to the saying of Ibn Umar: 'That is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry' and the Prophet saw a man intertwining his fingers in prayer, so he separated between his fingers. (And al-Takhassur (resting on hips)) due to the saying of Abu Hurayra: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade a man from praying while putting his hands on his hips' and it is narrated from him: that al-takhassur is the resting posture of the people of Hell. End.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rulings with proofs for four specific actions that fall under 'abath in prayer. The first is turning pebbles (or smoothing the ground) during prayer. The Hanafi ruling is that it is prohibited except if done once for prostration, based on multiple authentic hadith. The second is cracking fingers (making them pop). This is considered a forbidden act (makruh tahrimi) in prayer, as it is a type of 'abath and also resembles the actions of the people of Lot. Evidence includes direct prohibitions from the Prophet and the statement that those who laugh, look around, and crack fingers are equal in sin. Outside prayer, it is merely tanzihi (lightly disliked) unless done for a need. The third is intertwining fingers (tashbik). This is disliked because it resembles the prayer of those who have incurred Allah's anger. The hadith shows the Prophet himself separated the fingers of a man who intertwined them in prayer. The fourth is al-takhassur (placing hands on hips). This is prohibited based on a hadith of Abu Hurayra, and it is described as the posture of the people of Hell. The overall ruling for all these actions is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) due to the explicit prohibition and their being part of 'abath.

Key Terms
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa‘at al-asābi‘): Cracking or popping the finger joints to produce a sound.
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing hands on the hips during prayer, resembling the posture of the people of Hell.
عبث ('abath): Futile or unnecessary actions that distract from the solemnity of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on intertwining fingers (tashbik) during prayer?
A) Permissible because it is a natural resting position.
B) Makruh tahrimi because it resembles the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry.
C) Sunnah because the Prophet did it.
D) Haram and invalidates the prayer.

Key Takeaway
Four specific futile actions in prayer are prohibitively disliked based on explicit prophetic prohibitions and the principle of avoiding 'abath.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Disliked Actions in Prayer and Their Evidences
Rulings and proofs for disliked actions in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five disliked actions in prayer, state their rulings (makruh tahrimi or tanzih), and cite the hadith proofs for each.

Core Idea
Prayer is sacred; even minor distractions like flipping pebbles or cracking fingers break the spirit of devotion.

The Hanafi school pays meticulous attention to the perfection of prayer, not just its validity. This lesson explores several actions that are disliked (makruh) during salah, each supported by explicit hadith. You will learn not only the rulings but also the evidentiary basis and the degree of karahah, enabling you to avoid these distractions and deepen your khushu'.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked action
Read the Arabic passage and list all actions mentioned: flipping pebbles, cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips, turning the neck, and spitting. Note the specific conditions (e.g., flipping is allowed once for prostration).
2
Locate the hadith proof for each action
For each action, find the hadith cited. Note the source (e.g., Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud) and the key phrase that indicates prohibition or dislike.
Pay attention to the wording: 'la tamsah' (do not wipe), 'la tufarqi' (do not crack), 'nahaa' (forbade).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Determine how the hadith proves the ruling. For example, the imperative 'la taf'al' indicates prohibition, which in Hanafi usul yields karahah tahrimiyyah unless there is evidence to lower it to tanzih.
The text explicitly says the karahah is tahrimiyyah due to the prohibition and consensus.
4
Distinguish between tahrimi and tanzih
Note that some actions are makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) and others makruh tanzih (lightly disliked). For example, cracking fingers in prayer is tahrimi; outside prayer without need is tanzih. Spitting without necessity is tanzih if done to the left or under foot.
The degree depends on the strength of the evidence and the presence of a clear prohibition.
5
Apply the ruling to avoid these actions
Recognize that these actions do not invalidate prayer but reduce its reward and contradict the spirit of devotion. Commit to avoiding them in your own salah.
The common misunderstanding is that they nullify prayer; they do not, unless excessive (e.g., excessive turning may break prayer).

عليه الصلاة والسلام: « لا تمسح الحصى وأنتَ تُصَلِّي، فإنْ كنتَ لا بد فاعِلاً فواحدةً» (۲) متفق عليه، وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: « إِذَا قَامَ أَحَدُكُمْ فِي الصَّلاةِ فَلا يَمْسَحُ الْحَصَى فَإِنَّ (وفَرْقَعَةُ الأصابع) ولو مرة، وهو: أن يغمزهــا أو تُصَوّت، لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ( لا تفرقع أصَابِعَكَ وأنتَ فِي الصَّلاةِ » ) ولأنــه نوع من العبث، كذا في ( البرهان ). وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « الصَّاحِكُ فِي الصَّلاةِ والملتَفِتُ والمفرقع أصَابِعَهُ سواءٌ ( ) يعني : في الإثم كذا في مجمع الروايات » ، وإنمـا كــره لأنـه عـمـل قـوم لوط، فيكره التشبه بهم، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام لعلي ه: « إني أحـب لـك مـا أحـب لنفسي، لا تفرقع أصابعك وأنْتَ تُصَلِّي ) () كذا في المستصفى » ، وينبغي أن تكون الكراهة تحريمية للنهي الوارد في ذلك، ولأنها من أفراد العبث كذا في ( البحر ) ، والإجماع على كراهتها في الصلاة انتهى. كذا في « الدراية» بخلاف الفرقعة خارج الصلاة لغير حاجة فإنها تنزيهية، (وتشبيكها) لقول ابـن عمر فيه: « تِلْكَ صَلاةُ المغضوب عليهم ) ( ورأى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم رجلاً قد شبك أصابعه في الصلاة، ففرج عليه الصلاة والسلام بين أصابعه ) والتَّخَصُرُ) لقول أبي هريرة : ( نَهَى رسولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يصلي الرَّجُلَ مُختصراً ) وفي لفظ ( نهى عَنِ الاخْتِصَارِ في الصَّلاةِ » (۳) وهو أن يضع يده على خاصرته. قاله ابن سيرين وهو أشهر تأويلاتها، وهو الأصح ويؤيده ما أخرجه أبو داود عن زيـاد بـن صبيح الحنفي قال: «صليتُ إلى جنب ابن عمر فوضعت يدي على خاصرتي، فلما صلى قال: هذا الصَّلْبُ فِي الصَّلاةِ وكان رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينهى عنه ) (۳) وفي تفسير آخر وهو أن يتكـى عـلـى المخصرة، وهـي العصى وقيل: أن لا يتم الركوع والسجود، وقيل: أن يختصر الآيات التي فيها السجدة، ويكره أيضاً خارج الصلاة، فإن إبليس أخرج من الجنة متخصراً، والاختصار استراحة أهل النار أي: اليهود، وإنما يكره لمعنيين: ترك سنة أخذ اليدين والثاني: أنه من فعل الجبابرة، وقال في « البحر » : والذي يظهر أنها تحريمية فيها للنهي المذكور، والالتفاتُ بعُنُقِهِ ) لقول عائشة : « سألت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عــن التفاتِ الرَّجُل في الصَّلاةِ فقال : اختلاس يختلسه الشيطانُ مِن صَلاة الـعبــد » ) رواه البخاري. وقوله : ( لا يزالُ الله مقبلاً على العبد وهو في صلاته ما لم يلتفت، فإن التفت انصرف عنه» (ه). رواه أبو داود والنسائي، ويكره أن يرمي ببزاقه، والبزاق كغُراب: ماء الفم إذا خرج منه، ومادام فيـه فهو ريق، فتسميته بزاق باعتبار الأول، أو يرمي بنخامته بضم النون البلغم الذي ينفذ إلى الحلق بالنفس العنيف، إما من الخيشوم أو الصدر إذا لم يكن مدفوعاً إليه، وإن اضطر إليه فلا يكره الرمي، والأولى أن يأخذه بثوبه أو يلقيها تحت رجله اليسرى إذا لم يكن يصلي في المسجد، لما في البخاري أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : « إِذَا قَامَ أحَدُكُمْ إِلَى الصَّلاةِ فَلا يَنْصُقُ أَمَامَهُ، فَإِنَّمَا يُنَاجِي اللَّهَ مَا دَامَ فِي مُصَلاهُ وَلَا عَنْ يَمِينِهِ فَإِنَّ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ مَلَكينِ، وَلْيَبْصُقْ عَنْ يَسَارِهِ أَوْ تَحْتَ قَدَمِهِ » .
And flipping pebbles, except for prostrating once, and cracking the fingers, and interlocking them, and placing hands on hips. ... And flipping pebbles, except for prostrating once, due to his saying peace be upon him: 'Do not wipe pebbles while you are praying, but if you must, then once.' Agreed upon. And his saying peace be upon him: 'When one of you stands in prayer, do not wipe pebbles, for mercy faces him.' Narrated by the authors of the Sunan. And the saying of Jabir ibn Abdullah: 'I asked the Prophet peace be upon him about wiping pebbles, and he said: Once, and to refrain from it is better for you than a hundred black-eyed camels.' And (cracking the fingers) even once, which is pressing them or making them sound, due to his saying peace be upon him: 'Do not crack your fingers while you are in prayer.' And because it is a type of frivolity, as in al-Burhan. And his saying peace be upon him: 'The one who laughs in prayer, the one who turns away, and the one who cracks his fingers are equal,' meaning in sin, as in Majma' al-Riwayat. And it is disliked because it is the action of the people of Lot, so it is disliked to imitate them. He said peace be upon him to Ali: 'I love for you what I love for myself: do not crack your fingers while you are praying,' as in al-Mustasfa. And the karahah should be tahrimiyyah due to the prohibition that came concerning that, and because it is a form of frivolity, as in al-Bahr. And there is consensus on its karahah in prayer, as in al-Dirayah. Unlike cracking outside prayer without need, for it is tanzihiyyah. And (interlocking them) due to the saying of Ibn Umar concerning it: 'That is the prayer of those with whom Allah is angry.' And he saw the Prophet peace be upon him (interpreting?) a man who had interlocked his fingers in prayer, so he peace be upon him separated his fingers. And (placing hands on hips) due to the saying of Abu Hurayrah: 'The Messenger of Allah peace be upon him forbade that a man pray with his hands on his hips.' And in another wording: 'He forbade placing hands on hips in prayer.' It is that he places his hand on his waist. Ibn Sirin said that, and it is the most famous interpretation, and it is the most correct. It is supported by what Abu Dawud narrated from Ziyad ibn Subayh al-Hanafi: 'I prayed next to Ibn Umar and placed my hand on my waist. When he finished praying, he said: This is the cross (?) in prayer, and the Messenger of Allah peace be upon him used to forbid it.' In another interpretation, it is to lean on a staff. And it is said: that he does not complete ruku and sujud. And it is said: that he abbreviates the verses that contain sajdah. It is also disliked outside prayer, for Iblis was expelled from Paradise while placing his hand on his waist, and it is the resting posture of the people of Hell, i.e., the Jews. It is disliked for two reasons: leaving the sunnah of holding the hands, and the second: it is the action of tyrants. And he said in al-Bahr: what is apparent is that it is tahrimiyyah due to the mentioned prohibition. And (turning the neck) due to the saying of Aishah: 'I asked the Messenger of Allah peace be upon him about a man turning in prayer, and he said: It is something snatched away by Satan from the prayer of the servant.' Narrated by Bukhari. And his saying: 'Allah continues to turn to the servant while he is in prayer, as long as he does not turn away. If he turns away, He turns away from him.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i. And it is disliked to spit saliva. Saliva (al-bazaq) is like ghurab: the water of the mouth when it exits, and as long as it is inside it is called riq, so naming it bazaq is considering the first. Or to spit phlegm (nukhamah), which is the phlegm that reaches the throat by violent breathing, either from the nose or chest if not forced. If he is compelled, then spitting is not disliked. The preferable is to take it in his garment or put it under his left foot if he is not praying in the mosque. For in Bukhari that he peace be upon him said: 'When one of you stands to pray, he should not spit in front of him, for he is conversing with Allah as long as he is in his prayer place, nor to his right, for on his right is an angel, but he should spit to his left or under his foot.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah (a commentary on Nur al-Idah) discusses several actions that are makruh (disliked) during the prayer. The author lists them and provides the hadith evidence for each ruling, also noting the degree of karahah (tahrimee or tanzihi) where applicable.

1. Flipping pebbles (قلب الحصى): It is allowed only once if necessary for prostration, based on the hadith "Do not wipe pebbles while praying, but if you must, then once." The prohibition indicates karahah tahrimeeyah? The text quotes multiple hadith and the statement of Jabir.

2. Cracking fingers (فرقعة الأصابع): Even once is disliked, whether by pressing or making a sound. The hadith explicitly forbids it, and it is called frivolity. The text says the karahah is tahrimeeyah due to the prohibition, and consensus on its dislike in prayer is noted. Outside prayer without need, it is tanzih (lesser dislike).

3. Interlocking fingers (تشبيك الأصابع): Based on the hadith of Ibn Umar describing it as the prayer of the angered ones, and the Prophet separating a man's interlocked fingers. It is thus makruh.

4. Placing hands on the hips (التخصر): This is when a person places his hand on his waist. The Prophet forbade it. The author mentions multiple interpretations but the dominant one is placing hands on the waist. It is also disliked outside prayer. The text leans toward tahrimeeyah for this action.

5. Turning the neck (الالتفات بعنقه): Turning the face away from the qibla. The hadith calls it a snatching by Satan. It is makruh tahrimee? The text does not explicitly grade it but mentions severe warning.

6. Spitting (البزاق والنخامة): It is disliked to spit in front or to the right; one should spit to the left or under the foot, and if in the mosque, take it in a cloth. If compelled, it is not disliked.

The passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from hadith and shows the rigor of the school in preserving the sanctity of prayer.

Key Terms
قلب الحصى (qalb al-hasa): Flipping or moving pebbles on the ground during prayer, which is disliked except once if necessary for prostration.
فرقعة الأصابع (farqa'at al-asabi'): Cracking or popping the finger joints, either by pressing or making them sound.
تشبيك الأصابع (tashbik al-asabi'): Interlocking the fingers of one hand with the other during prayer.
التخصر (al-takhassur): Placing hand(s) on the hip during prayer, also called 'iktisar'.
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the face away from the qibla during prayer; a sinful distraction.
البزاق (al-bazaq): Saliva or spittle ejected from the mouth.
النخامة (al-nukhamah): Phlegm brought up from the throat or chest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for cracking one's fingers during prayer?
A) It is permissible but disliked (makruh tanzih).
B) It is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to the prohibition.
C) It invalidates the prayer completely.
D) It is recommended if one's joints are stiff.

Key Takeaway
Flipping pebbles, cracking fingers, interlocking fingers, placing hands on hips, turning the neck, and unnecessary spitting are all disliked in Hanafi fiqh; most are makruh tahrimi, and they do not nullify prayer but reduce its reward.

Ruling on Ikhtisar (Placing Hands on Hips) in Salah
Ruling with proof on ikhtisar using hadith and interpretations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of ikhtisar in salah according to the Hanafi school and explain the evidence and reasons for its prohibition.

Core Idea
Ikhtisar is makruh tahrimi in salah due to explicit prophetic prohibition; it resembles tyrants and the people of Hell.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi ruling on placing hands on the hips during prayer. It cites a hadith and various interpretations, ultimately concluding that the act is prohibitively disliked. Understanding this ruling helps you distinguish between degrees of prohibition and appreciate how the Hanafi school weighs textual evidence and contextual reasons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act (ikhtisar)
Define ikhtisar as placing hands on the waist/hips during prayer. Note that the most authoritative interpretation is from Ibn Sirin, supported by a narration from Ibn Umar.
The term 'ikhtisar' can also mean leaning on a staff or skipping sajdah verses, but the primary meaning here is hands on hips.
2
Identify the ruling (hukm)
The Hanafi relied-upon position is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). This is derived from the explicit prophetic prohibition (nahy) and the act's resemblance to tyrants and the posture of Iblis.
Makruh tahrimi is closer to haram but not at the same level; it requires repentance but does not invalidate the prayer.
3
Analyse the dalil (evidence)
The primary evidence is the hadith narrated by Abu Dawud where the Prophet forbade ikhtisar. The Hanafi school considers this prohibition as indicating karahah tahrimiyyah, not outright haram, because the context and additional reasons (resembling tyrants, Iblis) reinforce the prohibition without making it absolutely binding.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so ruling is makruh tahrimi, not haram.
4
Consider the reasons (ta'lil)
The text mentions two reasons: abandoning the sunnah of placing hands (right over left) and resembling tyrants (and by extension, Iblis). These reinforce the prohibition and explain why it is disliked even outside prayer.
The act is not invalidating but still prohibited due to these reasons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّجُلَ مُختصراً ) وفي لفظ ( نهى عَنِ الاخْتِصَارِ في الصَّلاةِ » (۳) وهو أن يضع يديه على خاصرته. قاله ابن سيرين وهو أشهر تأويلاتها، وهو الأصح ويؤيده ما أخرجه أبو داود عن زياد بن صبيح الحنفي قال: «صليتُ إلى جنب ابن عمر فوضعت يدي على خاصرتي، فلما صلى قال: هذا الصَّلْبُ في الصَّلاةِ وكان رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ينهى عنه ) (۳) وفي تفسير آخر وهو أن يتكـى عـلـى المخصرة، وهـي العصى وقيل: أن لا يتم الركوع والسجود، وقيل: أن يختصر الآيات التي فيها السجدة، ويكره أيضاً خارج الصلاة، فإن إبليس أخرج من الجنة متخصراً، والاختصار استراحة أهل النار أي: اليهود، وإنما يكره لمعنيين: ترك سنة أخذ اليدين والثاني: أنه من فعل الجبابرة، وقال في « البحر » : والذي يظهر أنها تحريمية فيها للنهي المذكور
The man [is]... and in a wording: 'He forbade ikhtisar in prayer' (3) and it is that he places his hands on his waist. Ibn Sirin said it, and it is the most famous of its interpretations, and it is the most correct. It is supported by what Abu Dawud narrated from Ziyad ibn Subayh al-Hanafi who said: 'I prayed next to Ibn Umar and placed my hands on my waist. When he finished prayer, he said: This is the cross in prayer, and the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) used to forbid it.' (3) And in another interpretation: that he leans on a staff (al-mikhsara), which is a stick. And it is said: that he does not complete the ruku' and sujud. And it is said: that he skips the verses containing sajdah. It is also disliked outside prayer, for Iblis was expelled from paradise in a state of ikhtisar, and ikhtisar is the repose of the people of Hell, i.e., the Jews. It is disliked for two reasons: abandoning the sunnah of taking the hands, and second: it is the action of the tyrants. He said in 'al-Bahr': 'It appears that it is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) due to the aforementioned prohibition.'
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on ikhtisar—placing hands on hips during prayer. The author begins by citing a hadith of the Prophet (hadith text) prohibiting ikhtisar. The most widely accepted interpretation, attributed to Ibn Sirin, is that it means placing hands on the waist. This is supported by a narration from Ziyad ibn Subayh al-Hanafi where Ibn Umar criticized the act. The author then mentions other interpretations: leaning on a staff, not completing ruku' and sujud, or skipping sajdah verses. Importantly, ikhtisar is also disliked outside of prayer, as it resembles the posture of Iblis and the repose of the Jews. The author, referencing 'al-Bahr' (likely al-Bahr al-Ra'iq), concludes that the karahah is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because the explicit prohibition makes it closer to haram. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الاختصار (al-ikhtisar): Placing one's hands on the waist/hips during prayer.
المخصرة (al-mikhsara): A staff or stick that one leans on.

C) Makruh tanzih
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Ikhtisar in salah is prohibited (makruh tahrimi) due to the hadith and its resemblance to undesirable postures.

The Ruling on Turning the Neck (Iltifat) in Prayer
Ruling with proof – cites hadiths to establish the prohibition of iltifat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iltifat (turning the neck) in prayer and identify the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Turning the neck in prayer is a snatching by shaytan — makruh tahrimi in the Hanafi school.

This passage from Nur al-Idah with its commentary provides the evidentiary basis for the ruling on iltifat (turning the neck/head) in salah. The author cites two hadiths: one from Aisha describing it as a snatching by shaytan, and another stating that Allah turns away from the one who turns. These prove the act is severely disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being discussed? What is the hukm according to the Hanafi school?
The act is iltifat bi 'unuq (turning the neck), not the entire chest.
2
Locate the dalil
Which two hadiths are cited? Note their sources (Bukhari, Abu Dawud, Nasa'i).
The first hadith uses the term 'ikhtilas' (snatching).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the phrase 'ikhtilas' indicate a prohibition? Why does it lead to makruh tahrimi and not haram?
The term implies theft, which is a strong prohibition, but the dalil is zanni.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Do other schools disagree? What is the practical difference?
Some schools consider it makruh tanzih or even haram.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
How does the ruling affect daily practice?
A Hanafi would avoid iltifat more strictly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والالتفاتُ بعُنُقِهِ ) لقول عائشة : « سألت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عــن التفاتِ الرَّجُل في الصَّلاةِ فقال : اختلاس يختلسه الشيطانُ مِن صَـلاة الـعبــد » ) رواه البخاري. وقوله : ( لا يزالُ الله مقبلاً على العبد وهو في صلاته ما لم يلتفت، فإن التفت انصرف عنه» (ه). رواه أبو داود والنسائي
And turning the neck: due to the saying of Aisha: 'I asked the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) about a man turning in prayer, so he said: It is a snatching that shaytan snatches from the prayer of the servant.' Narrated by Bukhari. And his saying: 'Allah continues to turn towards the servant as long as he is in his prayer as long as he does not turn away; if he turns away, He turns away from him.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and Nasa'i.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for the prohibition of turning the neck (iltifat bi-'unuq) during prayer. Two hadiths are cited: one from Aisha describing iltifat as 'a snatching by shaytan', indicating it is a serious offense; the second states that Allah turns away from the servant who turns away in prayer. These establish the karahah of iltifat. The author will later add conditions about when this applies.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the neck/head away from the qibla during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Iltifat (turning the neck) in prayer is makruh tahrimi because it is a snatching by shaytan, but it does not invalidate the prayer unless the chest turns.

Ruling on Spitting During Prayer
Explains the ruling on spitting or clearing phlegm during prayer and the proper method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on spitting during prayer, explain the hadith evidence, and apply the proper method.

Core Idea
Spitting in prayer is disliked but permissible when needed, and must be directed left or under the foot.

This passage from Nur al-Idah explains the ruling on spitting or clearing phlegm during prayer, distinguishing between spittle (bazaq) and phlegm (nukhamah). It provides the default ruling of karahah tanzihiyyah and the exception of compulsion, along with the prophetic guidance on where to spit. Understanding this ruling helps maintain proper adab in prayer while accommodating genuine needs.

Guided Path
1
Define the terms
Identify the difference between bazaq (spittle that leaves the mouth) and nukhamah (phlegm from chest/nose expelled forcefully). Note that as long as saliva is in the mouth it is called ree, not bazaq.
This linguistic distinction affects the ruling.
2
State the default ruling
The default ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful) to spit or expel phlegm during prayer. The karahah is due to the prohibition in the hadith and the fact that it conflicts with the state of conversation with Allah.
Makruh tanzih means it is better to avoid, but not sinful.
3
Identify the exception
If one is compelled (idtirar) — e.g., phlegm is bothering or choking — then throwing it is not disliked. The necessity removes the karahah.
Compulsion (darurah) overrides the dislike.
4
Apply the hadith evidence
The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade spitting in front or to the right during prayer, and instructed to spit to the left or under the left foot. In the mosque, spitting is a sin and must be buried.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim. The prohibition indicates karahah, not tahrim.
5
Specify the proper method
The author notes that 'throwing' (alqa) is deliberate: one should not produce sound with letters that would invalidate the prayer. It should be done without disrupting the prayer, preferably into a cloth or under the left foot if not in a mosque.
If spitting produces discernible letters, it invalidates the prayer (like talking).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكره أن يرمي ببزاقه، والبزاق كغُراب: ماء الفم إذا خرج منه، ومادام فيـه فهو ريق، فتسميته بزاق باعتبار الأول، أو يرمي بنخامته بضم النون البلغم الذي ينفذ إلى الحلق بالنفس العنيف، إما من الخيشوم أو الصدر إذا لم يكن مدفوعاً إليه، وإن اضطر إليه فلا يكره الرمي، والأولى أن يأخذه بثوبه أو يلقيها تحت رجله اليسرى إذا لم يكن يصلي في المسجد، لما في البخاري أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال : « إِذَا قَامَ أحَدُكُمْ إِلَى الصَّلاةِ فَلا يَنْصُقُ أَمَامَهُ، فَإِنَّمَا يُنَاجِي اللَّهَ مَا دَامَ فِي مُصَلاهُ وَلَا عَنْ يَمِينِهِ فَإِنَّ عَنْ يَمِينِهِ مَلَكينِ، وَلْيَبْصُقْ عَنْ يَسَارِهِ أَوْ تَحْتَ قَدَمِهِ » . وفي رواية: ( أو تَحْتَ قَدَمِهِ اليُسْرَى (). وفي الصحيحين: ( الْبُزَاقُ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ خَطِيئَةٌ وَكَفَّارَتُهَا دفنها ) (۲) انتهى. وإنما عبر بالإلقاء لأنه لا يلزم منه أن يكون بحروف فيفسد الصلاة فالمراد بـه الرمي بدون صوت به حروف عند الضرورة انتهى.
And it is disliked that he throws his spittle (bazaq) - and bazaq like ghurab: the water of the mouth when it leaves it, and as long as it is inside it is 'reeq', so its naming as bazaq considers the former. Or he throws his phlegm (nukhamah) - with damma on the noon - the phlegm that reaches the throat by forceful breathing, either from the nose or the chest if it is not forced to him. And if he is compelled to it, then throwing is not disliked, and it is better that he takes it in his garment or throws it under his left foot if he is not praying in the mosque. For in Bukhari that he (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you stands to pray, let him not spit in front of him, for he is conversing with Allah as long as he is in his prayer place, nor to his right, for on his right is an angel; let him spit to his left or under his foot.' And in a narration: 'or under his left foot.' And in the two Sahihs: 'Spitting in the mosque is a sin and its expiation is to bury it.' End. And he only expressed with 'throwing' because it does not necessitate that it be with letters that would invalidate the prayer, so what is meant is throwing without a sound with letters when necessary. End.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on spitting or expelling phlegm during prayer. It distinguishes between bazaq (spittle) and nukhamah (phlegm from chest/nose). The general ruling is karahah, but if one is compelled (e.g., phlegm is bothering them), it is not disliked. The author provides guidelines from hadith: do not spit forward or to the right (where angels are), but to the left or under the left foot if not in the mosque. In the mosque, spitting is a sin and must be buried. The author notes that 'throwing' (alqa) is used to indicate that it should not produce sound with letters, which would invalidate prayer. This ruling is based on authentic hadith.

Key Terms
البزاق (al-bazaq): Spittle, saliva that leaves the mouth.
النخامة (al-nukhamah): Phlegm that emerges from the throat or chest by forceful breathing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person must spit during prayer, where should they spit according to the hadith?
A) In front of them
B) To their right
C) To their left or under their left foot
D) Any direction except towards the qibla

Key Takeaway
Spitting during prayer is makruh tanzih but becomes permissible when necessary, and must be done to the left or under the foot without producing sound with letters.

Conditions for the Karahah of Iltifat and Exception for Turning the Face
Refines the ruling on iltifat, specifying conditions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible and impermissible turning (iltifat) in prayer according to the Hanafi school, stating the ruling (makruh tahrimi vs permissible) and the conditions that modify it.

Core Idea
Turning the neck in prayer without excuse is makruh tahrimi; with excuse, it is not disliked.

This lesson refines the general ruling on iltifat by specifying when it is actually disliked and when it is permissible. The key distinction lies in the presence of an excuse and the body part turned. The author draws on the Prophet's own practice to establish that turning with an excuse is not only allowed but sometimes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is iltifat bi al-'unuq (turning the neck). The default ruling is that it is makruh, but this is conditional.
The karahah is not absolute.
2
Apply the condition: excuse vs. no excuse
If there is a valid excuse (e.g., to check on followers), the act is not disliked. Without excuse, it remains makruh.
The excuse must be genuine, not trivial.
3
Distinguish neck from chest
Turning the chest (sadr) invalidates the prayer (mubtil), whereas turning only the neck does not invalidate but is disliked without excuse.
This is a critical Hanafi distinction.
4
Consider the evidence from the Prophet's action
The Prophet sometimes turned his face to check on his followers, establishing permissibility for a need. This is used as dalil.
The hadith 'he used to see from behind him as from in front' supports permissibility.
5
Determine the level of karahah
According to al-Bahr, the karahah is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) when without excuse, not just tanzihi.
Some earlier texts did not specify the level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد في ( الغاية كراهة الالتفات بالعنق بأن يكون بغير عذر، وأما تحويل الوجه لعذر فغير مكروه. قال صاحب ( البحر » : وينبغي أن تكون الكراهة تحريمية، وقيدنا بالعنق لأنه بالصدر مبطل وتقدم (۳)، وأما تحويل النظر يمنة ويسرة من غير تحويل الوجه فغير مكروه مطلقاً، كما سنذكره، والأولى تركه لأنه ينافي الأدب لغير حاجة، والظاهر أن فعله عليه الصلاة والسلام إياه كان لحاجة تفقد أحوال المقتدين مـع مـا فيـه مـن بـيـان الجواز، وإلا فهو صلى الله عليه وسلم « كان ينظر من خلفه كما ينظر أمامه » (ه)، كما في الصحيحين.
And it is restricted in al-Ghayah: the karahah of turning the neck is when there is no excuse. As for turning the face for an excuse, it is not disliked. The author of al-Bahr said: 'It should be that the karahah is tahrimi.' And we restricted it to the neck because turning with the chest invalidates, and it preceded (3). As for turning the glance right and left without turning the face, it is not disliked absolutely, as we will mention, but leaving it is better because it contradicts proper etiquette without need. And apparently his (peace be upon him) doing so was for the need to check the conditions of the followers, along with what it contains of demonstrating permissibility. Otherwise, he (peace be upon him) 'used to see from behind him as he saw from in front of him,' as in the two Sahihs.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage qualifies the earlier ruling on iltifat. The author, referencing al-Ghayah (likely Ghayat al-Bayan or similar), states that the karahah applies only when there is no excuse. If there is a valid reason to turn one's face, it is not disliked. The author of al-Bahr insists the karahah is tahrimi, not tanzih. Importantly, iltifat is restricted to turning the neck; turning the entire chest (sadr) invalidates the prayer entirely. Turning just the eyes or head without moving the face is not disliked at all, though it is better to avoid it. The Prophet himself would sometimes turn his face to check on his companions, indicating permissibility. Thus, the ruling is nuanced.

Key Terms
الالتفات بالعنق (al-iltifat bi al-'unuq): Turning the neck/head away from the qibla.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is turning the neck (iltifat bi-'unuq) in prayer considered makruh tahrimi?
A) Always
B) When done without a valid excuse
C) Only if it involves turning the chest
D) Only if done while standing

Key Takeaway
Turning the neck in prayer is not always disliked; it is only makruh tahrimi without a valid excuse, and turning the chest invalidates the prayer.

Prohibition of Iq'a' (Sitting Like a Dog) in Prayer
Defines iq'a' and cites hadiths forbidding it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-iq'a' and state the Hanafi ruling for it, citing the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Sitting on your heels with knees raised during prayer mimics a dog — and the Prophet forbade it.

This lesson explores the posture known as iq'a' — a sitting style that resembles a dog's posture. The Hanafi school rules it makruh tahrimi based on multiple hadiths. We'll learn exactly what iq'a' is, which definitions exist, and why it is disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what iq'a' is (the act) and what ruling applies. The preferred definition: placing buttocks on the ground and raising knees to the chest. The ruling is makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: three hadiths from Abu Hurayrah, Aisha, and Anas. All explicitly forbid iq'a' or compare it to dogs.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the hadiths prove the ruling. The word 'nahā' (forbade) indicates prohibition. In Hanafi usul, such a prohibition in a hadith (zanni al-thubut) establishes makruh tahrimi, not haram.
Hadiths are ahad; Quran or mutawatir would be needed for haram.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Recognize that some Hanafis consider it makruh tanzih, and other schools may hold it haram. The text supports the stronger tahrimi view.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is makruh tahrimi, based on the multiple explicit prohibitions. The tarjih authority is the cumulative weight of the hadiths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) يـكــره ( الإقعاء) وهو أن يضع اليتيه على الأرض وينصب ركبتيه وقيل : أن يعتمد بيديه على الأرض ويجمع الركبتين إلى صدره، وقيل: هو أن ينصب قدميه، كما يفعل في السجود ويضع إليتيـه علـى عقبيه ويضع يديه على الأرض والأول أصح، لأن إقعاء الكلب يكون بتلك الصفة، إلا أن إقعاء الكلب في نصب اليدين، وإقعاء الآدمي في نصب الركبتين إلى صدره، والأصل فيـه قـول أبـي هريرة له: «نَهَانِي رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَنْ نَفْرَةٍ كَنَفْرَةِ الدِّيكِ، وَإفْعَاء كَافْعَاءِ الْكَلْبِ وَالْتِفَاتِ كَالْتفات التَّعْلَبِ» ) رواه أحمد في مسنده». وقول عائشة كان تعني النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: ( ينهى عَنْ عَقَبَةِ الشَّيْطَانِ، وأنْ يَفْتَرِ الرَّجُلُ ذِرَاعَيْهِ افْتِرَاسُ السَّبْعِ " رواه البخاري. وعقبة الشيطان: الإقعاء، وقول أنس: قال لي النبي : ( إذا رَفَعْتَ رَأسَكَ مِنَ السُّجُودِ فَلا تُقع كَمَا يَفْعِي الكَلْبُ، ضَعْ الْبَيَكَ بِينَ قَدَمَيْكَ،
(And) it is disliked (al-iq'a'): and it is that he places his buttocks on the ground and sets up his knees. And it is said: that he supports himself with his hands on the ground and brings his knees to his chest. And it is said: that he sets up his feet, as in sujud, and places his buttocks on his heels and places his hands on the ground. The first is more correct, because the sitting of a dog is with that description, except that the sitting of a dog is in the setting up of the hands, and the sitting of a human is in the setting up of the knees to his chest. The basis for it is the saying of Abu Hurayrah: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade me from a leap like the leap of a rooster, and from iq'a' like the iq'a' of a dog, and from turning like the turning of a fox.' Narrated by Ahmad in his Musnad. And the saying of Aisha, meaning the Prophet: 'He forbade from the heel of shaytan, and that a man spread his arms like the spreading of a beast.' Narrated by Bukhari. And 'uqbat al-shaytan' is al-iq'a'. And the saying of Anas: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to me: When you raise your head from sujud, do not sit like a dog sits; place your buttocks between your feet.'
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage discusses the disliked act of iq'a', which is a sitting posture during prayer. Three definitions are given: (1) sitting on the ground with knees up, (2) supporting on hands, or (3) sitting on heels with feet upright like in sujud. The first definition is preferred because it matches a dog's posture, though the dog sits on its hands while humans sit on knees. The text then cites several hadith: Abu Hurayrah reports a prohibition of iq'a' (dog-like sitting), a rooster-like leap, and fox-like turning. Aisha reports prohibition of 'the heel of shaytan' (which is iq'a') and spreading arms like a beast. Anas reports direct instruction from the Prophet to avoid iq'a' when sitting between prostrations. Based on these explicit prohibitions, the Hanafi school rules iq'a' as makruh tahrimi (some say makruh tanzih, but the stronger view is tahrimi due to the strong prohibitions).

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iq'a'): A sitting posture in prayer where one sits on the heels with knees upright, resembling a dog's sit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the ruling for iq'a' (sitting like a dog) according to the preferred Hanafi position in this lesson?
A) Haram
B) Makruh tahrimi
C) Makruh tanzih
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Iq'a' is forbidden in prayer; the Hanafi school holds it makruh tahrimi, and the correct sitting posture is to avoid raising the knees like a dog.

Ruling on Turning the Neck (Iltifat) in Prayer
Details the ruling on turning the neck and its degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each type of iltifat in prayer (turning neck, chest, eyes) and identify when turning is excused.

Core Idea
Turning the neck without excuse is makruh tahrimi; turning the chest invalidates; shifting eyes is permissible.

In prayer, the concept of iltifat (turning) has specific rulings depending on which part of the body moves. This lesson examines the Hanafi classification of turning the neck, chest, and eyes, and the proof behind these distinctions. We will also explore the opinion of Sahib al-Bahr that turning the neck without excuse is prohibitively disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of iltifat
List the three types of turning mentioned in the passage: turning the neck (bil-'unuq), turning the chest (bis-sadr), and shifting the eyes (bi al-nazar).
The passage restricts discussion to the neck first, then distinguishes the chest and eyes.
2
State the ruling for each type
Turning the neck without excuse: makruh tahrimi; with excuse: not disliked. Turning the chest: invalidates the prayer. Shifting the eyes: not disliked at all, but better to avoid without need.
The ruling for the chest is mentioned as 'mubtil' (invalidating), referenced from an earlier section.
3
Locate the dalil
The Prophet (ﷺ) used to see from behind as from the front (Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim). This hadith shows he could see without turning, so turning without need is not required.
The hadith is cited to prove that turning for a need is permissible but not the norm.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith's indication (dalala) is zanni: it shows permissibility for need but implies dislike when done without need, especially since the Prophet's own turning was for a specific purpose.
The Hanafi school uses this to classify turning without excuse as makruh tahrimi.
5
Note the tarjih
Sahib al-Bahr (in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) clarifies that the karahah is tahrimi, not tanzihi. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the later Hanafi school.
The base text (Nur al-Idah) only says 'makruh', but the commentary specifies the degree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد في ( الغاية كراهة الالتفات بالعنق بأن يكون بغير عذر، وأما تحويل الوجه لعذر فغير مكروه. قال صاحب « البحر » : وينبغي أن تكون الكراهة تحريمية، وقيدنا بالعنق لأنه بالصدر مبطل وتقدم، وأما تحويل النظر يمنة ويسرة من غير تحويل الوجه فغير مكروه مطلقاً، كما سنذكره، والأولى تركه لأنه ينافي الأدب لغير حاجة، والظاهر أن فعله عليه الصلاة والسلام إياه كان لحاجة تفقد أحوال المقتدين مع ما فيه من بيان الجواز، وإلا فهو صلى الله عليه وسلم « كان ينظر من خلفه كما ينظر أمامه »، كما في الصحيحين.
And it is restricted in al-Ghaya: turning the neck is disliked if without excuse. As for turning the face with an excuse, it is not disliked. The author of al-Bahr said: The dislike should be prohibitive (tahrimi). We restricted it to the neck because turning the chest invalidates, as preceded. As for shifting the eyes right and left without turning the face, it is not disliked at all, as we will mention, but it is better to leave it because it contradicts proper etiquette without need. Apparently, the Prophet's doing so was out of need to check on the followers' conditions, along with showing permissibility. Otherwise, he used to see from behind as he saw from the front, as in the two Sahihs.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling on iltifat (turning) in prayer. The author first restricts the ruling from al-Ghaya: turning the neck without excuse is makruh. Then he clarifies that turning the face with an excuse is not disliked. The author of al-Bahr (a fiqh work) opines that the karahah is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). The restriction to the neck is because turning the chest invalidates the prayer (as previously stated). Turning the eyes without turning the face is not disliked at all, though it is better to avoid it unnecessarily. The Prophet's turning in prayer was for a need, and he could see from behind as from the front, as narrated in the Sahihain.

Key Terms
الالتفات (al-iltifat): Turning the neck in prayer; here distinguished from turning the chest (invalidating) and shifting eyes (not disliked).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is permissible without any dislike.

Key Takeaway
Turning the neck without excuse is makruh tahrimi; turning the chest invalidates; shifting the eyes is permissible but better avoided.

Ruling and Definition of Iq'a' in Prayer
Defines iq'a' and establishes its ruling as makruh tahrimi using multiple hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define iq'a' according to the Hanafi school, state its ruling as makruh tahrimi, and cite the prophetic evidence that supports this ruling.

Core Idea
Iq'a' in prayer is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) because it resembles the posture of animals and is forbidden by the Prophet.

Prayer postures are not merely physical; they carry spiritual and legal significance. This lesson dissects a specific sitting posture known as iq'a', which some may inadvertently adopt. Through the definitions and hadith cited, you will understand why the Hanafi school classifies it as makruh tahrimi—a prohibition close to haram—and how to avoid it in your practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Locate the term 'iq'a' in the source and list the three definitions given. Determine which definition is considered most correct (al-awwal asahh) and why.
The preferred definition is sitting on the buttocks with knees raised, resembling a dog.
2
Extract the ruling from the matn and commentary
The matn states 'yukrah' (is disliked). The commentator adds 'wa hiya karahah tahrim' (it is a prohibitive dislike). Note the difference between makruh tanzih and tahrimi.
This classification means the act is sinful but does not invalidate the prayer.
3
Examine the hadith proofs
Read the three hadith (Abu Hurairah, Aisha, Anas). Identify the specific phrases that indicate prohibition: 'nahani' (he forbade me), 'yinha' (he forbids), 'la tuq'i' (do not squat).
The multiple authentic chains strengthen the evidence.
4
Understand the linguistic clarification
Note the discussion on the word 'iliyyatayhi' and the correction to 'iliyayhi'. This shows attention to precise language in Hanafi texts.
The feminine ta is dropped in idafa, as in 'khusyayhi'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And (it) is disliked (al-iq'a'), which is to place the buttocks on the ground and raise the knees; or it is said: to support oneself with the hands on the ground and bring the knees to the chest; or it is said: to stretch the feet as done in prostration and place the buttocks on the heels and place the hands on the ground. The first is more correct, because a dog's iq'a' is of that description, except that the dog's iq'a' involves stretching the forelegs, while a human's iq'a' involves raising the knees to the chest. The basis for it is the saying of Abu Hurairah: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade me from a leap like the leap of a rooster, and sitting like a dog, and turning like a fox' (Ahmad in his Musnad). And the saying of Aisha, meaning the Prophet: 'He forbade from the sitting of the devil, and that a man spread his forearms like a beast of prey' (Bukhari). 'The sitting of the devil' is iq'a'. And the saying of Anas: 'The Prophet said to me: When you raise your head from prostration, do not squat like a dog; place your feet between your feet, and stick the backs of your feet to the ground' (Ibn Majah). And it is a prohibitive dislike (karahah tahrim). Al-'Aqbah (with damma and sukun) and al-'Aqab (with fatha and sukun) mean iq'a', as in al-Bahr from al-Mughrib. And his saying 'iliyyatayhi' in al-Hidayah: some say he should say 'iliyayhi' not 'iliyyatayhi' because the feminine ta is dropped upon idafa; you say 'khusyayhi' not 'khusyatayhi'. As the poet said: 'Her buttocks quiver like the quivering of a waterskin.' And he said: 'Like his testicles from dangling' – as in al-'Uyun.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a detailed parsing exercise on iq'a', defining it and providing its ruling from multiple hadith. The author first gives the matn ruling: iq'a' is disliked. Then defines it with three opinions, preferring the first (buttocks on ground, knees up like a dog). Hadith from Abu Hurairah explicitly forbids iq'a' along with other acts. The hadith of Aisha calls it 'the sitting of the devil' and links it to spreading forearms. The hadith of Anas provides practical guidance after rising from prostration. The author concludes that the karahah is tahrimi (prohibitive dislike). He also clarifies linguistic issues about the word 'iliyatayn' (buttocks).

Key Terms
الإقعاء (al-iq'a'): Sitting on the buttocks with knees raised, resembling a dog; also includes other postures like squatting.
عقبة الشيطان (uqbat al-shaytan): Another term for iq'a', meaning 'the sitting of the devil'.
إليتيه (iliyyatayhi): Dual of 'iliyyah' meaning buttocks; corrected in the source to 'iliyayhi' due to idafa.

Iq'a' in prayer is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) due to explicit prophetic prohibition and resemblance to animal postures.

Disliked Acts in Prayer: Forearms, Sleeves, Sirwal, and Salam Gesture
Lists multiple disliked acts with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state four disliked acts in prayer according to the Hanafi school and identify the hadith evidence or reasoning for each.

Core Idea
Rolling up sleeves, spreading forearms, praying in trousers while able to wear a shirt, and returning salam with gestures are all disliked.

This lesson examines four actions that are considered makruh (disliked) during prayer. Each has a basis in the Sunnah or the principle of avoiding carelessness. Understanding these helps refine one's prayer and avoid what may reduce its reward or, in the case of handshake, invalidate it.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked act
The passage lists four distinct acts: iftirash al-dhira'ayn (spreading forearms), tashmir al-kummayn (rolling up sleeves), praying in sirwal/izar while able to wear a shirt, and radd al-salam bi-ishara (responding to salam with a gesture).
Each is labeled as 'yukrah' (it is disliked).
2
Note the evidence for each
For tashmir, the hadith 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to tuck up hair or garment' is cited. For iftirash, the previous hadith is referenced. For sirwal, the reasoning is carelessness. For salam, the text notes the danger of invalidation if shaking hands intentionally.
The hadith for iftirash was narrated earlier (Aisha's hadith).
3
Understand the underlying principle
The prohibition of tucking clothes (kaff) applies to rolling up sleeves. Spreading forearms is disliked because it resembles beasts. Praying in minimal clothing while able to wear more shows disrespect. Returning salam with gesture is a 'salam in meaning' and can invalidate if combined with a handshake.
The principle of khushu' is central.
4
Distinguish between disliked and invalidating
All are disliked, but the salam gesture has a conditional invalidation: if the person shakes hands with the intention of returning the greeting, the prayer becomes invalid. Nodding the head is permissible according to an athar from Aisha.
This nuance is often overlooked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) يكره (افتراش ذراعَيْهِ) لما رويناه ) ، (وَ) يكره (تشميرُ كُمَّيْهِ عنهما ) لقوله : « أُمِرْتُ أَنْ أَسْجُدَ عَلَى سَبْعَةِ أَعْظُم وَأنْ لا أكُفَّ شَعَرَا وَلا ثَوْبـا مـتـفـق عـلـيـه، وهـو يتضمن كراهة تشمير الكمين، ولما فيه من الجفاء المنافي للخشوع؛ (وَصَلَاتُهُ في السراويل) أو في الإزار (مَعَ قُدْرَتِهِ عَلَى لُبْسِ القَمِيصِ ) لما فيه من التهاون والتكاسل وقلة الأدب، والمستحب للرجل أن يصلي في ثلاثة أثواب : إزار وقميص وعمامة، وللمرأة أن تصلي في قميص وخمار ومقنعة)، كمـا البرهان»، و«مجمع الروايات»، (وَ) يكره (رَدُّ السَّلامِ بِالإِشَارَةِ)؛ لأنه سلام معنى، حتّـى لـو صافح بنيته تفسد ، كما قدمناه ، وقال في « الذخيرة لا بأس للمصلي أن يجيب المتكلم برأسه، بــه ورد الأثر عن عائشة : ولا بأس بأن يتكلَّمَ الرَّجلُ معَ المصلي.
(And) it is disliked (spreading his forearms) because we narrated it; (and) it is disliked (rolling up his sleeves from them) due to his saying: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to tuck up hair or garment' (agreed upon). This includes dislike of rolling up sleeves, and because it contains harshness contrary to humility. (And his praying in sirwal [trousers]) or in izaar (waist-wrapper) (while being able to wear a shirt) because it shows carelessness, laziness, and lack of etiquette. It is recommended for a man to pray in three garments: izaar, shirt, and turban; for a woman to pray in a shirt, khimar, and miqna'ah, as in al-Burhan and Majma' al-Riwayat. (And) it is disliked (responding to salam with a gesture) because it is a salam in meaning; even if he shakes hands with intention, it invalidates, as we have stated. And it is said in al-Dhakhirah: There is no problem for the praying person to respond to a speaker with his head; a report from Aisha supports it. And there is no problem for a man to speak with the praying person.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage expands on several disliked acts in prayer. First, spreading the forearms (iftingash adh-dhira'ayn) is disliked, based on the hadith of Aisha previously cited. Second, rolling up the sleeves (tashmir al-kummayn) is disliked because the Prophet was commanded not to tuck hair or garments during prostration (muttafaq alayh), and it implies harshness contrary to khushu'. Third, praying in sirwal (trousers) or izaar while able to wear a shirt is disliked due to carelessness. The recommended minimum for a man is three garments: izaar, shirt, and turban; for a woman: shirt, khimar, and miqna'ah. Fourth, responding to the salam with a gesture (not verbally) is disliked because the salam is a greeting in meaning; if he shakes hands intentionally, the prayer is invalidated. However, the praying person may respond to a speaker by nodding the head, as per an athar from Aisha, and there is no harm in someone speaking to the person in prayer.

Key Terms
افتراش الذراعين (iftirash al-dhira'ayn): Spreading the forearms on the ground during prostration like a beast of prey.
تشمير الكمين (tashmir al-kummayn): Rolling up the sleeves before prayer.
رد السلام بالإشارة (radd al-salam bi al-ishara): Responding to the greeting of salam by hand gesture or head movement instead of verbal reply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible if one nods the head.
B) It is disliked, and shaking hands with intention invalidates prayer.
C) It is prohibited and invalidates prayer.
D) It is recommended to respond verbally.

Key Takeaway
Four acts are disliked in prayer: spreading forearms, rolling up sleeves, praying in trousers while able to wear a shirt, and responding to salam with a gesture; the latter invalidates if one shakes hands intentionally.

Sitting Cross-Legged in Prayer: The Ruling of Karahah Tanzihiyyah
Ruling with proof: explain why sitting cross-legged is disliked in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sitting cross-legged in prayer without excuse and distinguish between the ruling inside and outside prayer.

Core Idea
Sitting cross-legged in prayer is mildly disliked because it abandons the sunnah posture, but it is not sinful.

In this lesson, we examine the posture of sitting cross-legged (tarbu') during prayer. The Hanafi school holds that while the posture itself is not forbidden, performing it in prayer without a valid reason is disliked (makruh tanzihi) because it means leaving the sunnah position. Outside prayer, it is perfectly fine, as the Prophet himself used to sit that way. Let's explore the textual evidence and reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sitting cross-legged (tarbu') during prayer. State the Hanafi ruling: makruh tanzihi when done without a valid excuse.
The ruling is not found for outside prayer; it is mubah (permissible).
2
Understand the reason for dislike
The dislike is due to abandoning the sunnah posture of sitting in prayer (the iftirash or tawarruk). Identify the sunnah posture from your prior knowledge.
The text says 'ترك سنة القعود فيها' (abandoning the sunnah of sitting in it).
3
Distinguish inside vs outside prayer
Recognize that the same posture is not disliked outside prayer. The proof given is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and Umar ibn al-Khattab often sat cross-legged in their regular sitting.
This difference shows that the disliked nature is specific to the context of prayer.
4
Recognize the nature of the karahah
The commentator states that the karahah in prayer is tanzihiyyah (lightly disliked). Understand what that means: it is better to avoid, but not sinful, and the act is still valid.
This distinguishes it from makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) which would be closer to haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُكْرَهُ التَّرَبُّعُ بِلَا عُذْرٍ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ تَرْكِ سُنَّةِ الْقُعُودِ فِيهَا، وَلَيْسَ بِمَكْرُوهٍ خَارِجَهَا، لِأَنَّ جُلَّ قُعُودِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم كَانَ التَّرَبُّعَ، وَكَذَا عُمَرُ بْنُ الْخَطَّابِ، وَهُوَ يُفِيدُ أَنَّ الْكَرَاهَةَ فِي الصَّلَاةِ تَنْزِيهِيَّةٌ، وَسُمِّيَ تَرَبُّعاً: لِأَنَّ صَاحِبَ هَذِهِ الْجِلْسَةِ قَدْ تَرَبَّعَ نَفْسَهُ، كَمَا يُرَبَّعُ الشَّيْءُ إِذَا جُعِلَ أَرْبَعاً، وَالْأَرْبَعُ هُنَا: السَّاقَانِ وَالْفَخِذَانِ، رَبَّعَهَا بِمَعْنَى: أَدْخَلَ بَعْضَهَا تَحْتَ بَعْضٍ.
And sitting cross-legged without excuse is disliked; because it involves abandoning the sunnah of sitting in prayer, but it is not disliked outside prayer, because most of the Prophet's sitting was cross-legged, and similarly Umar ibn al-Khattab. This indicates that the dislike in prayer is tanzihi (lightly disliked). It is called tarbu' because the person sitting makes himself four-cornered (like a square), just as one makes something four. The four are the two shins and two thighs, 'ruba' means to insert some under others.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of sitting cross-legged (tarbu') during prayer. The matn states that it is disliked (makruh) when done without a valid excuse. The sharh explains that the reason for dislike is that it involves leaving the sunnah posture of sitting (i.e., iftirash or tawarruk as per sunnah). However, outside of prayer, sitting cross-legged is not disliked because the Prophet (peace be upon him) himself used to sit cross-legged often, as did Umar. This indicates that the disliked nature is tanzihi (i.e., makruh tanzihi, not tahrimi). The term 'tarbu' is derived from 'arba' (four) because the sitter's legs and thighs are arranged in a four-cornered manner.

Key Terms
التَّرَبُّع (al-tarbu'): Sitting with legs crossed, thighs and shins arranged in a four-cornered posture.
الْكَرَاهَةَ التَّنْزِيهِيَّةَ (al-karahah al-tanzihiyyah): Lightly disliked, meaning it is better to avoid but not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on sitting cross-legged (tarbu') in prayer without a valid excuse according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is haram.
B) It is makruh tanzihi.
C) It is mubah.
D) It is sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Sitting cross-legged in prayer without excuse is makruh tanzihi because it leaves the sunnah posture, but it does not invalidate the prayer and is permissible outside prayer.

Ruling on Plaiting Hair in Prayer (ʿAqs)
Ruling on plaiting hair in prayer with definitions and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on plaiting hair during prayer and identify the proofs from hadith.

Core Idea
Plaiting hair prevents it from prostrating, so it is makruh in prayer.

This lesson examines the specific ruling on ʿaqs (tying hair) during salah. The Hanafi school considers it makruh, based on clear hadith evidence. We'll explore the definitions, the proofs, and the underlying wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
First, understand what ʿaqs means: tying, plaiting, or fastening hair so it cannot hang freely. The matn states it is disliked (yukrahu). In Hanafi terminology, this is generally makruh tahrimi due to the strong prohibitions in the hadith.
The text gives several definitions; the core is preventing hair from spreading during prostration.
2
Examine the Dalil
Three hadith proofs are cited: (1) Ibn Abbas untying a man's hair, quoting the Prophet's comparison to being tied up (maktuf). (2) The Prophet's prohibition: 'Do not tie your hair in prayer, for it is the seat of Shaytan.' (3) The Prophet passing by a man with tied hair and saying 'Let your hair prostrate with you.'
All three hadith show clear disapproval.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The reasoning: hair is part of the body that should be in a natural state during sujud, touching the ground. Tying it prevents this, contradicting the spirit of humility and full prostration. The analogies (tied up, seat of shaytan) indicate it is disliked.
The hadith are explicit, so the ruling is based on text, not mere opinion.
4
Synthesize the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi school holds that ʿaqs is makruh (prohibitively disliked). The prayer is still valid, but one should avoid it. The wisdom is to ensure all limbs, including hair, participate in prostration.
No fundamental disagreement among Hanafis; all consider it makruh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ) يُكْرَهُ (عَقْصُ شَعْرِهِ) وَهُوَ شَدُّ ضَفِيرَتِهِ حَوْلَ رَأْسِهِ، كَمَا تَفْعَلُهُ النِّسَاءُ وَالتُّرْكُ وَخُدَّامُ الْأُمَرَاءِ، كَمَا فِي «الْحَافِظِيَّةِ». وَقِيلَ: جَمْعُهُ عَلَى هَامَتِهِ وَشَدُّهُ بِخَيْطٍ، أَوْ صَمْغٍ لِيَتَلَبَّدَ. وَقِيلَ: لَيُّهُ وَإِدْخَالُ أَطْرَافِهِ فِي أُصُولِهِ. وَقِيلَ: شَدُّهُ عَلَى الْقَفَا لِئَلَّا يُصِيبَ الْأَرْضَ إِذَا سَجَدَ. وَذَلِكَ لِمَا فِي مُسْلِمٍ: «أَنَّ عَبْدَ اللهِ بْنَ عَبَّاسٍ رَأَى عَبْدَ اللهِ بْنَ الْحَارِثِ يُصَلِّي وَرَأْسُهُ مَعْقُوصٌ مِنْ وَرَائِهِ، فَقَامَ وَرَاءَهُ فَجَعَلَ يَحُلُّهُ، فَلَمَّا انْصَرَفَ أَقْبَلَ عَلَى ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ فَقَالَ: مَا لَكَ وَلِرَأْسِي؟ قَالَ: إِنِّي سَمِعْتُ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم يَقُولُ: (إِنَّمَا مِثْلُ هَذَا مَثَلُ الَّذِي يُصَلِّي وَهُوَ مَكْتُوفٌ)» وَلِقَوْلِ عَلِيٍّ رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لَا تَعْقِصْ شَعْرَكَ فِي الصَّلَاةِ فَإِنَّهُ كِفْلُ الشَّيْطَانِ»، رَوَاهُ عَبْدُ الرَّزَّاقِ. وَفِي شَرْحِ الْغَزْنَوِيِّ: لِأَنَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ مَرَّ بِرَجُلٍ يُصَلِّي وَهُوَ مَعْقُوصُ الشَّعْرِ فَقَالَ: «دَعْ شَعْرَكَ يَسْجُدُ مَعَكَ».
And plaiting his hair is disliked: that is, tying his braid around his head as women, Turks, and servants of princes do. It is also said: gathering it on his crown and tying it with thread or gum to make it matted. Or: twisting it and inserting its ends into its roots. Or: tying it at the back of the neck to prevent it from touching the ground when prostrating. This is due to the hadith in Muslim: 'Abdullah ibn 'Abbas saw 'Abdullah ibn al-Harith praying with his hair tied behind him, so he stood behind him and began to undo it. When he finished, he turned to Ibn 'Abbas and said, 'What is it with you and my hair?' He said, 'I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say, 'This is like the one who prays while tied up.' And due to 'Ali's statement: 'The Messenger of Allah said, 'Do not tie your hair in prayer, for it is the seat of Shaytan.' And in Sharh al-Ghaznawi: Because he (peace be upon him) passed by a man praying with his hair tied and said, 'Let your hair prostrate with you.'
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on 'aqs (tying hair) during prayer. The matn states it is disliked, and the sharh provides multiple definitions and proofs. The core evidence includes the hadith where Ibn Abbas untied a man's hair, comparing it to being 'tied up' (maktuf), and the hadith that tying hair makes it a 'seat of Shaytan'. The wisdom is that hair should prostrate with the worshiper. Thus, it is makruh to prevent hair from touching the ground during sujud.

Key Terms
عَقْص ('aqs): Tying or plaiting hair and fastening it so it cannot spread during prostration.
مَكْتُوف (maktuf): Bound or tied up, used as an analogy for the state of hair being restricted.
كِفْل الشَّيْطَان (kifl al-shaytan): Seat or resting place of Shaytan, indicating the act is disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a form of adornment.
B) It prevents the hair from prostrating with the worshiper.
C) It causes distraction.
D) It is impolite.

Key Takeaway
Plaiting hair ('aqs) during prayer is makruh because it prevents the hair from prostrating with the worshiper, as proven by clear hadith.

Ruling on I'tijar (Wrapping Head Leaving Crown Exposed) in Prayer
Ruling on disliked head-wrapping in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on i'tijar in prayer, explain the reasons for its disliked status, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding its definition.

Core Idea
I'tijar is makruh in prayer because the Prophet prohibited it.

In this lesson, we examine i'tijar, a specific way of covering the head that leaves the crown exposed. The Hanafi school considers it disliked (makruh) during prayer. We will explore its definition, the evidence from hadith, and the reasoning behind the ruling, including a subtle difference among the Hanafi imams.

Guided Path
1
Define i'tijar linguistically and technically
Understand the two definitions: (1) tying the head with a handkerchief or turban leaving the middle exposed; (2) covering the nose with the turban (face-veil).
The second definition is attributed to Imam Muhammad.
2
Identify the ruling (makruh)
State the hukm: makruh tanzih. Understand why it is not haram despite the prohibition.
In Hanafi usul, nahi (prohibition) can indicate tahrim or karahah depending on qara'in (circumstantial evidence). Here it indicates karahah.
3
Examine the proof
The dalil is the Prophet's prohibition of i'tijar in prayer. This is a khabar wahid, so its thubut is zanni. The prohibition is understood as makruh tanzih.
The exact hadith is not quoted in the passage but referenced from Sharh al-Dayri.
4
Analyse the rationale
The reasons given: resemblance to women (tashabbuh bi al-nisa') or to rough bedouins. These are considered inappropriate during prayer.
The rationale helps lower the ruling from haram to makruh.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance
Imam Muhammad restricts i'tijar to cases where the nose is also covered (with face-veil). The mufta bihi is the general view including both definitions.
The practical difference: under Muhammad's view, wrapping without nose-cover may not be i'tijar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُكْرَهُ الِاعْتِجَارُ، وَهُوَ شَدُّ الرَّأْسِ بِالْمَنْدِيلِ، أَوْ تَكْوِيرُ عِمَامَتِهِ عَلَى رَأْسِهِ وَتَرْكُ وَسَطِهَا مَكْشُوفاً، وَقِيلَ: أَنْ يَتَنَقَّبَ بِعِمَامَتِهِ فَيُغَطِّيَ أَنْفَهُ، إِمَّا لِلْحَرِّ أَوْ لِلْبَرْدِ أَوْ لِلتَّكَبُّرِ، فَيَصِيرُ شِبْهَ الْمُعْجِزِ. وَعَنْ مُحَمَّدٍ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ: لَا يَكُونُ الِاعْتِجَارُ إِلَّا مَعَ تَنَقُّبٍ. وَذَلِكَ لِنَهْيِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عَنِ الِاعْتِجَارِ فِي الصَّلَاةِ، كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ الدَّيْرِيِّ. وَقَالَ فِي شَرْحِ الْمُنْيَةِ: وَرُبَّمَا يَكُونُ وَجْهُ الْكَرَاهَةِ التَّشَبُّهَ بِالنِّسَاءِ، أَوْ لِكَوْنِهِ فِعْلَ الْجُفَاةِ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ.
And i'tijar is disliked: it is tying the head with a handkerchief, or winding his turban on his head and leaving its middle exposed. It is also said: that he wears a face-veil with his turban and covers his nose, either due to heat or cold or out of arrogance, becoming like one who is helpless. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): i'tijar is only when accompanied by wearing a face-veil. This is due to the Prophet's (peace be upon him) prohibition of i'tijar in prayer. In Sharh al-Munya: perhaps the reason for dislike is imitation of women, or because it is the practice of rough bedouins.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage addresses i'tijar, which is wrapping the head in a way that leaves the crown exposed or covering the nose with the turban. The ruling is that it is disliked (makruh). The proofs include a general prohibition from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the rationale given is that it resembles women's attire or the manner of uncivilized bedouins. Imam Muhammad's view restricts i'tijar to cases where the nose is also covered. The dislike is due to the prohibition and the imitation of improper conduct.

Key Terms
الاعْتِجَار (al-i'tijar): Wrapping the head with a cloth or turban leaving the middle exposed, or covering the nose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is sunnah.
B) It is makruh.
C) It is mubah.
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
I'tijar is makruh in prayer because the Prophet prohibited it, and it resembles the dress of women or bedouins.

Ruling on Folding Garment (Kaff al-Thawb) in Prayer
Ruling on the disliked nature of folding or gathering the garment during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on folding the garment during prayer, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the underlying rationale of avoiding arrogance.

Core Idea
Folding your garment in prayer is disliked because it contradicts the hadith and shows arrogance.

In this lesson, we examine a specific disliked action in prayer: kaff al-thawb, which involves folding or gathering the garment to prevent it from spreading during prostration. The Hanafi school rules it as makruh based on a clear prophetic command and the principle that prayer demands humility, not pride. We will unpack the evidence from the hadith and understand why this action undermines the spirit of salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the text: the act is kaff al-thawb (folding the garment). The ruling is makruh (disliked). Note the specific definitions: raising it in front or behind, or tying it around the waist.
Makruh here refers to a disliked action that does not invalidate the prayer but reduces its reward.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garment.' This is mutaffaq 'alayh (agreed upon by Bukhari and Muslim).
This hadith is a direct prohibition (nahy) from the Prophet ﷺ.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'not to fold garment' is a clear command (amr) in the negative form, which in Hanafi usul indicates prohibition (tahrim). Since the prohibition is not absolutely definitive, it is karahah tahrimiyyah (close to haram).
The word 'ألا أكف' is a direct prohibition.
4
Identify the underlying rationale (illah)
The author adds: 'because it involves arrogance contradicting the essence of prayer, which is humility and submission.' This illah (cause) ties the ruling to the spirit of khushu'.
Even if one does not intend pride, the action resembles it.
5
Confirm the hukm level
Based on the dalil and illah, the Hanafi school rules it as makruh (disliked). Some later scholars consider it makruh tahrimi (close to forbidden), but the operative view is makruh.
There is no ikhtilaf among the four schools; all consider it disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيُكْرَهُ كَفُّ ثَوْبِهِ، أَيْ: رَفْعُهُ بَيْنَ يَدَيْهِ أَوْ مِنْ خَلْفِهِ إِذَا أَرَادَ السُّجُودَ. وَقِيلَ: أَنْ يَجْمَعَ ثَوْبَهُ وَيَشُدَّهُ فِي وَسَطِهِ. لِمَا قَدَّمْنَاهُ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ: (أُمِرْتُ أَنْ أَسْجُدَ عَلَى سَبْعَةِ أَعْظُمٍ وَأَنْ لَا أَكُفَّ شَعَراً وَلَا ثَوْباً) مُتَّفَقٌ عَلَيْهِ، وَلِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّجَبُّرِ الْمُنَافِي لِوَضْعِ الصَّلَاةِ، وَهُوَ الْخُشُوعُ وَالْخُضُوعُ.
And folding his garment is disliked: that is, raising it in front of him or behind him when he wants to prostrate. It is also said: that he gathers his garment and ties it around his waist. Due to what we previously mentioned of his statement: 'I have been commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garment' (agreed upon). And because it involves arrogance contradicting the essence of prayer, which is humility and submission.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses kaff al-thawb, which means folding or gathering the garment to prevent it from spreading during prostration. The ruling is that it is disliked (makruh). The evidence is the famous hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) was commanded to prostrate on seven bones and not to fold hair or garment. Additionally, such action shows a form of pride or vanity, which is contrary to the humble state of prayer. The hadith is mutaffaq 'alayh (agreed upon by Bukhari and Muslim).

Key Terms
كَفُّ ثَوْبِهِ (kaff al-thawb): Folding or gathering the garment to prevent it from spreading during prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is folding the garment (kaff al-thawb) disliked in prayer according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is a form of adornment.
B) It contradicts the command to prostrate with all bones and shows pride.
C) It is a distraction.
D) It is impolite.

Key Takeaway
Folding the garment during prayer is disliked because of the prophetic prohibition and because it contradicts the humility required in salah.
