And closing his eyes, and raising them to the sky, and stretching, and little action, and taking a louse and killing it... and [the text] 'al-Tabyin', and said in 'al-Bahr': placing the hand is established in Muslim and the sleeve is analogy. And in 'al-Khulasa': if he can take his lips with his teeth then he should do so, and place the back of his hand on his mouth, as in 'Mukhtarat al-Nawazil', and it should be with his right hand, and it is said: in standing and other than it with his left, likewise in 'al-Mujtaba'. (And) disliked is closing his eyes, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'When one of you stands in prayer, let him not close his eyes', [and because it contradicts] khushu', and it contains a type of frivolity, and leaving looking at the sunnah place, and because every limb and part has a share in this worship, so too the eye. And the dislike should be tanzihi if without necessity and no benefit; but if he fears losing khushu' due to seeing something that disturbs the mind, then closing them is not disliked, rather perhaps it is better, because then it is perfection of khushu' – said by the author of al-Bahr. (And) disliked is raising them to the sky, due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'What is the matter with people who raise their eyes to the sky? They must desist or their eyesight will be snatched away' – as in al-Burhan. (And) stretching, because it is from laziness and it contradicts khushu'. (And) disliked is little action that contradicts prayer, and its individual instances are many, like plucking one hair or two hairs, and the difference between little and much action preceded. And Qadi Khan considered from it a single shot from a bow in the prayer of fear, and it is not performed except with two hands, and perhaps because in that situation it is not thought that he is not in prayer, since walking was permitted for him, so too the shot due to his need for it. (And) from it is taking a louse and killing it without excuse; for if a louse or flea distracts him or bites him, then taking is not disliked. And in 'Majma' al-Riwayat': it is disliked to take a louse and kill it, but he should bury it under pebbles. And in the saying of Abu Hanifa and narrated from him: if he takes a louse or buries it, he has done evil – end. And in 'al-Bazzaziyya': The Imam said: burying it is more beloved to me than killing it, and Muhammad said: killing it, and the second said: both are disliked – end. And covering his nose and mouth. And in 'al-Tajnis': from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him: that killing a louse in prayer is more beloved to me than burying it, and all that is fine. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: he does not kill the louse in prayer but buries it under pebbles, due to what was narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, may Allah be pleased with him, that he was praying and took a louse and buried it, then recited the verse: 'Did We not make the earth a container?' [al-Mursalat 77:25]. And the justification for killing is that it removes harm from oneself, so there is no problem, like killing a snake or scorpion – end. And he said in al-Tajnis: also refraining from killing the louse is better – end. I said: and its justification is because it involves leaving action and avoiding the different opinion on impurity. Al-Jalal al-Suyuti said in 'al-Yanbu'': al-Zarkashi said: Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, disliked killing fleas and lice in the mosque, and al-Nawawi stated that if he kills them, he may not throw them in the mosque because they are carrion. And in the Musnad of Ahmad from Abu Ayyub said: 'A man found a louse in his garment, so he took it and threw it in the mosque, so the Messenger of Allah said to him: Do not do that; return it to your garment until you leave the mosque.' And Ibn al-Imad said: as for throwing lice in the mosque, if dead it is forbidden due to its impurity, and if alive, in the books of the Malikis it is forbidden to throw lice alive contrary to fleas, and the difference is that the flea lives by eating soil, unlike the louse; in throwing it there is torture by hunger, and that is not permissible, and he said: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything; so when you kill, kill well.' Based on this, it is forbidden to throw lice alive in the mosque and elsewhere, and it is forbidden for a man to throw his clothes while they have lice before killing them, and the better is not to kill it in the mosque, due to the hadith: 'When one of you finds a louse in his garment, let him wrap it and not throw it in the mosque' – narrated by Ahmad. And if he kills lice in his clothes and leaves them dead in them and prays in them, his prayer is not valid, because the carrion of lice and fleas are impure according to the correct view contrary to al-Qaffal, and a little of its blood in the garment is pardoned, even if he intentionally killed it, unlike skin because there is no difficulty in avoiding it. And if the blood of fleas appears on the mat of the mosque from someone sleeping in the mosque, concerning pardon for the one praying there is consideration, because avoidance is possible. And the sleeper should be ordered to place a barrier between himself and the mat while sleeping, to honor the sanctity of the mosque and protect its mat from being soiled by blood – end of the words of al-Jalal al-Suyuti. And it is explicitly stated in our books that it is not permissible to throw the shell of a louse in the mosque. (And) disliked is covering his nose and mouth, because we narrated that he forbade wrapping (sadl) and that...

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary (sharh) on Nur al-Idah, listing various disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer. The author first mentions the general list: closing eyes, raising eyes to the sky, stretching (tamattu), little actions, and taking/killing lice. He then expands on each ruling with proofs and scholarly discussion.

For closing the eyes: The hadith quoted is weak according to Hanafi scholars, but the dislike is based on it being contradicting khushu' and involving frivolity. The author clarifies that the dislike is tanzihi (not strictly prohibited) unless there is a necessity, such as fear of losing concentration due to distracting sights; then closing the eyes is not only permissible but preferable to preserve khushu'.

Raising eyes to the sky: A strong hadith (Bukhari) threatens loss of eyesight, so it is makruh. Stretching (tamattu) is from laziness and contrary to khushu'.

Little actions: Defined broadly, like plucking one or two hairs. The distinction between little and much action is discussed elsewhere. Qadi Khan included a single shot from a bow in the fear prayer because it is necessary and does not negate prayer.

Taking and killing lice is a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute. Abu Hanifa prefers burying the louse, citing a hadith of Ibn Mas'ud, while Muhammad prefers killing it (removing harm, analogy to snake/scorpion). Abu Yusuf said both are disliked. The author notes that killing is cleaner, but burying avoids impurity differed upon. The passage then extends to rulings about lice in the mosque, quoting Malik and others, concluding that throwing dead or alive lice in the mosque is forbidden, and one should wrap it and remove it from the mosque.

The passage ends with covering nose and mouth, but the text is cut off. It mentions a hadith forbidding sadl (wrapping) and covering, likely referring to covering the face with a garment while praying, which is also makruh.

Key Terms
قملة (qamlah): A louse; its killing and disposal during prayer is subject to ikhtilaf
دفن القملة (dafn al-qamlah): Burying the louse under pebbles or earth during prayer

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi method for a louse during prayer is to bury it under pebbles rather than kill it, to avoid impurity and scholarly dispute.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Killing Lice in Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Rulings and Proofs
Intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab discussion on killing lice in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing lice in prayer, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, and identify the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
Killing lice in prayer is permissible but refraining is better; dead lice are impure and cannot be thrown in the mosque.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah expands on the rulings concerning lice during prayer, covering the Hanafi school's internal difference and the proofs from the Salaf. It also addresses the impurity of dead lice and the etiquette of disposing of them, with inter-madhab comparisons. Understanding this nuanced ruling helps the student appreciate how the Hanafi school accommodates multiple valid proofs while providing a clear mufta bihi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing a louse during prayer. According to the Hanafi school, it is permissible (mubah), but refraining is better (afdal). Note the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa prefers burying without killing; Imam Muhammad prefers killing.
The ruling is based on the concept of removing harm (izalat al-adha), which is generally recommended.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for permissibility is the action of Ibn Mas'ud, who buried a louse during prayer and recited the Quranic verse about the earth being a receptacle. The proof for refraining is the hadith commanding to return a louse to the garment rather than throwing it in the mosque, implying it is better not to kill it.
Both proofs are from the Sunnah (ahad) and are zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
From Ibn Mas'ud's action: he buried the louse, not killed it, indicating that removing the louse from the body is permissible, but killing is not necessarily the only means. The hadith: 'Put it back in your garment' implies that the louse should not be killed and thrown, but kept alive until leaving the mosque. This points to the betterment of refraining from killing.
The Hanafi jurists weighed the harm caused by the louse vs. the harm of killing (which creates impurity) and concluded refraining is better.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa prefers burying; Imam Muhammad permits killing and considers it better. Externally: Malik dislikes killing in the mosque; some Shafi'is (al-Qaffal) consider dead lice pure, while Hanafis consider them impure.
The dispute on impurity affects the validity of prayer if dead lice remain in clothes.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that killing is permissible but refraining is better. This is based on the hadith and the consideration of avoiding the disputed impurity. The author cites al-Tajnis for the preferred view.
The Hanafi school gives weight to the safer option (ihtiyat) when proofs are balanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجنيس: وعن محمد رحمه الله: أن قتل القملة في الصلاة أحب إلي من دفنها، وكل ذلك لا بأس به، قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: لا يقتل القملة في الصلاة ويدفنها تحت الحصى، لما روي عبد الله بن مسعود الله أنه كان يصلي فأخذ قملة ودفنها، ثم تلا قوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ تَجَعَلِ الْأَرْضَ كِفَاتًا﴾ [المرسلات: 25] ووجه القتل أن فيه إزالة الأذى عن نفسه، فلا به بأس، كقتل الحية والعقرب انتهى. وقال في التجنيس: أيضاً الكف عن قتل القملة أفضل. انتهى، قلت: ووجهه لما فيه من ترك العمل والاحتراز عن النجاسة المختلف فيها، قال الجلال السيوطي في الينبوع: قال الزركشي كره مالك رحمه الله تعالى قتل البراغيث والقمل في المسجد، وصرح النووي بأنه إذا قتلها لا يجوز إلقاؤها في المسجد لأنها ميتة، وفي مسند أحمد عن أبي أيوب قال: وجد رجل في ثوبه قملة فأخذها فطرحها في المسجد، فقال له رسول الله: لا تفعل ردها في ثوبك حتى تخرج من المسجد (رواه أحمد) وقال ابن العماد: وأما طرح القمل في المسجد فإن كان ميتاً حرم لنجاسته، وإن كان حياً ففي كتب المالكية أنه يحرم طرح القمل حياً بخلاف البرغوث، والفرق أن البرغوث يعيش بأكل التراب، بخلاف القمل ففي طرحه تعذيب له بالجوع وهو لا يجوز، وقد قال: إن الله كتب الإحسان على كل شيء فإذا قتلتم فأحسنوا القتلة. وعلى هذا فيحرم طرح القمل حياً في المسجد وغيره، ويحرم على الرجل أن يلقي ثيابه وفيها قمل قبل قتله، والأولى أن لا يقتله في المسجد. لحديث: إذا وجد أحدكم القملة في ثوبه فليصرها ولا يطرحها في المسجد (رواه أحمد). ولو قتل القمل في ثيابه وتركه فيها ميتاً وصلى فيها لم تصح صلاته، لأن ميتة القمل والبرغوث نجسان على الصحيح خلافاً للقفال، ويعفى عن قليل دمها في الثوب، وإن تعمد قتلها بخلاف الجلد فإنه لا مشقة في التحرز منه ولو حصل على حصر المسجد دم البراغيث ممن ينام في المسجد ففي العفو عنه بالنسبة إلى المصلي نظراً، لأن التحرز عنه ممكن، وينبغي أن يؤمر النائم بأن يجعل بينه وبين الحصير حائلاً حالة النوم، تعظيماً لحرمة المسجد وحفظاً لحصره عن تنجسها بالدم انتهى عبارة الجلال السيوطي رحمه الله تعالى. والمصرح به في كتبنا أنه لا يجوز إلقاء قشر القملة في المسجد
In al-Tajnis: From Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him): that killing a louse in prayer is more beloved to me than burying it, and all that is not objectionable. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Do not kill the louse in prayer, but bury it under pebbles, because it is narrated from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that he was praying, took a louse and buried it, then recited the verse: 'Have We not made the earth a receptacle?' [al-Mursalat:25]. The reasoning for killing is that it removes harm from oneself, so there is no problem, like killing a snake or scorpion. End quote. And he said in al-Tajnis: Also, refraining from killing the louse is better. End quote. I say: Its reasoning is because it involves leaving action and avoiding the disputed impurity. Al-Jalal al-Suyuti said in al-Yanbu': al-Zarkashi said: Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) disliked killing fleas and lice in the mosque, and al-Nawawi explicitly stated that if one kills them, it is not permissible to throw them in the mosque because they are dead. In Musnad Ahmad, from Abu Ayyub, he said: A man found a louse in his garment, took it, and threw it in the mosque. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to him: 'Do not do that; put it back in your garment until you leave the mosque.' (Ahmad) Ibn al-Imad said: As for throwing lice in the mosque, if it is dead, it is prohibited due to its impurity; and if it is alive, in the Maliki books it is prohibited to throw live lice, unlike fleas. The difference is that the flea lives by eating dirt, unlike the louse; in throwing it there is torture by hunger, which is not permissible. And He (the Prophet) said: 'Indeed Allah has prescribed excellence in everything; when you kill, kill well.' Based on this, it is prohibited to throw live lice in the mosque and elsewhere, and it is prohibited for a man to throw his garment while it has lice before killing them. It is better not to kill it in the mosque, based on the hadith: 'When one of you finds a louse in his garment, let him tie it and not throw it in the mosque.' (Ahmad). If one kills lice in his clothes and leaves them dead in them and prays in them, his prayer is not valid, because the dead louse and flea are impure according to the correct view, contrary to al-Qaffal. A small amount of its blood in the garment is pardoned, even if he deliberately killed it, unlike skin, because there is no difficulty in avoiding it. If blood of fleas gets on the prayer mat of someone who sleeps in the mosque, regarding its pardon for the one praying, there is consideration because it is possible to avoid it. It is proper that the sleeper be ordered to place a barrier between himself and the mat while sleeping, to honor the sanctity of the mosque and preserve its mats from being soiled with blood. End of the statement of al-Jalal al-Suyuti (may Allah have mercy on him). And it is clearly stated in our books that it is not permissible to throw the shell of a louse in the mosque.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on the ruling of killing lice during prayer and related matters. The author begins by quoting from al-Tajnis, where Imam Muhammad considers killing a louse in prayer more beloved than burying it, though both are permissible. Abu Hanifa prefers burying without killing, based on a narration from Ibn Mas'ud who buried a louse during prayer and recited the verse about the earth being a receptacle. The rationale for killing is that it removes harm, analogized to killing a snake or scorpion. However, al-Tajnis also states that refraining from killing is better, which the sharh author explains is to avoid unnecessary action and the disputed impurity of the louse. The author then extensively quotes al-Suyuti, who reports Malik's dislike of killing fleas and lice in the mosque, and al-Nawawi's prohibition of throwing dead lice in the mosque due to impurity. A hadith from Ahmad instructs returning a louse to the garment rather than throwing it in the mosque. The discussion includes the Maliki view that throwing live lice is prohibited because it causes suffering, unlike fleas. The preferred view is not to kill lice in the mosque. The passage concludes that according to Hanafi texts, even the shell of a louse may not be thrown in the mosque, and further that if one prays with dead lice in his clothes, the prayer is invalid because dead lice and fleas are impure (najis) by the correct view, contrary to al-Qaffal. A small amount of blood is pardoned, but there are conditions regarding blood on the prayer mat.

Key Terms
قملة (qamlah): a louse, a small insect that infests hair and clothes
كِفَاتًا (kifatan): receptacle, that which collects or holds (referring to the earth in the verse)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is forbidden (haram)
B) It is permissible but better to refrain
C) It is obligatory to kill it
D) It invalidates the prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits killing lice in prayer but prefers refraining; dead lice are impure, so they should not be thrown in the mosque.

Makruh Acts in Prayer: Covering, Prostration Conditions, and Prohibited Locations
List of makruh acts in prayer with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on several makruh acts in prayer, distinguish between tanzih and tahrim karahah, and identify when prostration becomes invalid due to barriers.

Core Idea
Prostration requires forehead contact with the ground; covering the nose and mouth or praying in impure places is makruh with varying severity.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah outlines several disliked actions during salah, detailing both the ruling (makruh tanzih or tahrim) and the evidence. Some actions, like prostrating solely on the forehead without the nose, involve leaving a wajib and thus carry a stronger prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and rulings for covering and placing objects
From the text, 'covering his nose and mouth' and 'placing something in his mouth that prevents the sunnah recitation' are both makruh. Understand that these are makruh tanzih unless they lead to missing a wajib.
The proof for covering mouth is based on the prohibition of sadl.
2
Analyze prostration on the turban
The text states it is makruh unless there is an excuse (cold, rough ground). However, the critical point is that if the forehead does not touch the ground due to the turban, the prayer is invalid. The karahah here is tanzih because the Prophet and companions prostrated on turbans to show permissibility.
Ibn Amir Hajj clarifies the condition: validity requires forehead contact.
3
Examine prostration on a picture and restricting to the forehead
Prostration on a picture of a living creature is makruh tanzih due to resemblance to idol worship. Restricting sujud to the forehead without the nose (without excuse) is makruh tahrim because it omits the wajib of including the nose.
The tahrim designation is due to leaving a wajib.
4
Understand the prohibition of praying in specified locations
The text lists seven places from a hadith (garbage dump, slaughterhouse, graveyard, road, bath, camel resting places, roof of Kaaba). The karahah here is tanzih, as the prayer is valid but disliked due to obstruction or impurity.
The hadith is narrated by Ibn Majah and Tirmidhi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
، (وَ) يكره (تغطية أنفه، وفمه) أن يغَطِّيَ الرَّجُلُ فَاهُ ) ، (وَ) يكره (وَضعُ شيءٍ) في فَمِهِ وهو يمنع القراءة المسنونة، (وَ) يكره (السُّجودُ على كَوْرِ عِمَامَتِهِ) من غير ضرورة برد أو خشونة الأرض، وكور: دور، يقال: كار العمامة وكورها أدارها على رأسه، وهذه العمامة عشرة أكوار وعشرون كوراً، كذا في «المغرب» وهو بفتح الكاف كما ضبطه ابن أمير حاج قال البخاري في صحيحه: قال الحسن: كان القوم يسجدون على العمامة والقلنسوة، ولأنه حائل لا السجود فيجوز، كالخف والنعل، وقد نبه العلامة ابن أمير حاج ها هنا تنبيهاً حسناً وهو: أن صحة السجود على الكور محله إذا كان على الجبهة أو بعضها، أما إذا كان على الرأس فقط، وسجد عليه ولم تصب جبهته الأرض، فإن الصلاة لا تصح؛ لعدم السجود على محله، وكثير من العوام يتساهل في ذلك فيظن الجواز، والظاهر أن الكراهة تنزيهية؛ لنقل فعله وأصحابه من السجود على العمامة تعليماً للجواز، فلم تكن تحريمية، وقد أخرج أبو داود أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رجلاً وهو قد اعتم على جبهته فحسر عن جبهته إرشاداً لما هو الأفضل والأكمل، (و) يكره السجود ( على صورة) حيوان؛ لأنه يشبه عبادتها، (وَ) يكره (الاقتصارُ على الجبهة) في السجود (بلا عذر بالأنف)، لترك الواجب، وهو : ضم الأنف إليها، والكراهة: تحريمية، (وَ) تكره (الصلاة في الطريق)؛ لأن فيه منع الناس عن المرور وشغله بما ليس له؛ لأنها حق العامة للمرور، (و) في (الحَمَّامِ)، (وَفي المَخْرَج)، أي: الكنيف، (وَفي المَقْبَرَةِ) وفي أمثالها، لما رواه ابن ماجه والترمذي عن ابن عمر أن رسول الله نهى أن يصلى في سبعة مواطن في المزبلة والمجزرة والمقبرة وقارعة الطريق وفي الحمام ومعاطن الإبل وفوق ظهر بيت الله انتهى.
(And) it is disliked) covering his nose and mouth) that a man covers his mouth), (and) it is disliked (placing something) in his mouth that prevents the recommended recitation, (and) it is disliked (prostrating on the wrapping of his turban) without [؟] necessity of cold or roughness of ground. And 'kawr' means a roll, one says: 'karra al-‘amama' and 'kawwara' means he wound it on his head. This turban has ten rolls and twenty rolls. Thus in al-Mughrib, and it is with fatha on the kaf as Ibn Amir Hajj recorded it. al-Bukhari said in his Sahih: al-Hasan said: The people used to prostrate on the turban and the cap. And because it is a barrier that does not [prevent?] prostration, it is permissible, like the socks and sandals. And the scholar Ibn Amir Hajj gave here a good note: that the validity of prostration on the roll is when it is on the forehead or part of it; but if it is only on the head and he prostrates on it and his forehead does not touch the ground, then the prayer is not valid, due to absence of prostration on its place. Many laymen are negligent in this and think it is permissible. The apparent is that the dislike is tanzihi (non-prohibitive), because the action of the Prophet and his companions of prostrating on the turban was transmitted to teach permissibility, so it was not tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). And Abu Dawud narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw a man who had tied his turban on his forehead, so he uncovered his forehead, guiding to what is better and more perfect. (And) it is disliked (prostrating on a picture) of an animal, because it resembles its worship. (And) it is disliked (restricting to the forehead) in prostration (without excuse, [leaving] the nose), because leaving an obligatory (act), which is including the nose with it. And the dislike is tahrimi. (And) it is disliked (praying on the road), because it prevents people from passing and occupies it with what is not for it, because it is a public right for passage. (And) in (the bath), (and in the toilet), i.e., the latrine, (and in the graveyard) and similar places, because of what Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah forbade praying in seven places: the garbage dump, the slaughterhouse, the graveyard, the main road, the bath, the camel resting places, and on the roof of the House of Allah. End.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage lists several makruh acts in prayer with their proofs. The author states that covering the nose and mouth in prayer is makruh, based on the hadith forbidding sadl (covering the body with a single garment) and that a man should not cover his mouth. Next, placing something non-melting in the mouth that prevents the sunnah recitation is makruh. Prostrating on the wound part of the turban is makruh unless there is an excuse like cold or rough ground. The author explains that the dislike is tanzihiyyah because the Prophet and his companions prostrated on turbans, showing permissibility; however, if the turban prevents the forehead from touching the ground, the prayer is invalid. Prostrating on a picture of a living creature is makruh because it resembles worship of the image. Restricting prostration to the forehead without including the nose is makruh tahriman because it is wajib to also place the nose. Finally, prayer in the road, bath, toilet, graveyard, and similar places is makruh based on the hadith from Ibn Umar listing seven prohibited locations. The karahah here is generally tanzihiyyah unless the act involves obstruction or disrespect.

Key Terms
كراهة تنزيهية (karahah tanzihiyyah): A disliked act that is not sinful; avoiding it is better but it is permissible.
السدل (al-sadl): Covering oneself with a cloth in prayer such that the hands are covered
كراهة تحريمية (karahah tahrimiyyah): A prohibited dislike; closer to haram but not necessarily impermissible; often arises from leaving a wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Makruh tahrimi
B) Makruh tanzihi
C) Haram
D) Valid without dislike

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between actions that are mildly disliked (tanzih) and those that are prohibited (tahrim), based on whether a wajib is omitted or the act resembles impermissible behavior.

Disliked Acts: Covering Mouth and Placing Objects in Mouth During Prayer
Ruling statement on two disliked acts in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for covering the mouth and placing non-dissolving objects in the mouth during prayer, and identify the underlying reason for the dislike.

Core Idea
Covering the mouth or placing a non-dissolving object in the mouth during prayer is makruh because it hinders Sunnah recitation or distracts the mind.

In this passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah, we learn about two specific makruhat in prayer: covering the mouth and placing an object in the mouth that does not dissolve, which either prevents the Sunnah recitation or occupies the mind. These rulings illustrate how actions that compromise the focus or perfection of prayer are discouraged in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Locate the two actions explicitly mentioned: (1) covering the mouth, (2) placing a non-dissolving object in the mouth that prevents Sunnah recitation or distracts.
The text uses 'يكره' to indicate the ruling.
2
Understand the conditions for dislike
Note the conditions: the object must not dissolve, and it must either prevent the Sunnah recitation (Qur'an reading in prayer) or occupy the mind (distract).
If the object dissolves, the ruling may differ.
3
Derive the ruling and reason
Conclude that both actions are makruh. The underlying reason is that they hinder the proper performance of prayer by affecting recitation or concentration.
The recitation is sunnah (opening verses, surahs after Fatiha), so preventing it is disliked.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووضع شيءٍ في فَمِهِ يمنعُ القراءة المسنونة، يمنع يُغَطِّيَ الرَّجُلُ فَاهُ ) ، (وَ) يكره (وَضعُ شيءٍ) لا يذوب (في فَمِهِ) وهو (يمنع القراءة المسنونة)، أو يشغل باله كذهب وفضة وحجر
And placing something in his mouth that prevents the Sunnah recitation. He prevents: the man covers his mouth. And (it is) disliked (to place) something that does not dissolve (in his mouth) which (prevents the Sunnah recitation), or occupies his mind, like gold, silver, and stone.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Nur al-Idah. It addresses two disliked actions (makruhat) in prayer: first, covering the mouth; second, placing a non-dissolving object in the mouth that either prevents the recitation of the Qur'an (which is sunnah) or distracts the mind, such as gold, silver, or a stone. The underlying reason is that these actions hinder the proper performance of the prayer. The ruling is that such acts are makruh.

Key Terms
المســــــنونة (al-masnunah): The recitation of the Qur'an in prayer, which is a sunnah (recommended) act according to the Hanafi school.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Disliked; an act that is discouraged but not prohibited. In this context, it refers to tanzihi (slightly disliked) or tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Covering the mouth or placing a non-dissolving object in the mouth that prevents Sunnah recitation or distracts is makruh in prayer, as it hinders the proper performance of the prayer.

Prostrating on the Turban Fold: Ruling and Condition
Detailed commentary on the ruling of prostrating on the turban's fold

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prostrating on the turban fold, identify the condition that invalidates the prayer, and explain why the dislike is tanzihi.

Core Idea
Prostration on the turban fold is valid but disliked; the forehead must touch the ground.

This lesson examines a common scenario in prayer: what if a person prostrates while wearing a turban, specifically with the fold (kawr) on the forehead? Is the prayer valid? Is it disliked? The passage provides a nuanced answer, distinguishing between valid but disliked (tanzihi) and invalid when the forehead is entirely covered. Understanding this ruling prevents invalid prayers and refines one's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its primary ruling
The act is prostrating on the fold of the turban without necessity. The primary ruling is that it is disliked (makruh). Note the exception: if there is necessity like heat, cold, or rough ground, it is not disliked.
2
Understand the definition of 'kawr'
Al-kawr is a fold or twist of the turban wound around the head. The text mentions turbans may have multiple folds (e.g., ten or twenty).
Knowing this helps apply the ruling correctly.
3
Recognize the critical condition for validity
Ibn Amir Hajj clarifies: if the turban fold is on the forehead (or part of it), prostration is valid. But if the turban is only on the head and the forehead does not touch the ground, the prayer is invalid because the obligatory forehead prostration is missing.
Many common people are careless about this.
4
Examine the evidence for permissibility and dislike
The text cites al-Hasan al-Basri that the Prophet's companions prostrated on turbans and caps, showing permissibility. Also, Abu Dawud reports the Prophet uncovered a man's forehead who had wound the turban on his forehead, guiding to what is more perfect. This indicates the dislike is not prohibitive.
5
Classify the dislike: tanzihi vs tahrimi
The commentator concludes the dislike is tanzihi (non-prohibitive) because the Prophet and companions themselves prostrated on turbans, teaching permissibility. The Prophet's action of uncovering is only for the more perfect practice, not to forbid.
Some scholars hold it is makruh tahrimi, but the Hanafi view is tanzihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) يكره (السُّجودُ على كَوْرِ عِمَامَتِهِ) من غير ضرورة حر، أو برد، أو خشونة الأرض، والكور: دور، يقال: كار العمامة وكورها أدارها على رأسه، وهذه العمامة عشرة أكوار وعشرون كوراً، كذا في «المغرب» وهو بفتح الكاف كما ضبطـه ابـن أمـيـر حـاج قـال البخاري في صحيحه » : قال الحسن: كانَ القوم يسجدون على العمامة والقلنسوة"، ولأنه حائل لا السجود فيجوز، كالخف والنعل، وقد نبه العلامة ابن أمير حاج ها هنا تنبيهاً حسناً وهو: أن صحة السجود على الكور محله إذا كان على الجبهة أو بعضها، أما إذا كان على الرأس فقط، وسجد عليه ولم تصب جبهته الأرض، فإن الصلاة لا تصح؛ لعدم السجود على محله، وكثير من الـعـوام يتساهل في ذلك فيظن الجواز، والظاهر أن الكراهة تنزيهية؛ لنقل فعله وأصحابه من السجود على العمامة تعليماً للجواز، فلم تكن تحريمية، وقد أخرج أبو داود « أَنَّ رسولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم رَأَى رَجُلاً وَهُوَ قَدْ اعْتَمَّ عَلَى جَبْهَتِهِ فَحسرَ عَنْ جَبهَته ) (۳) إرشاداً لما هو الأفضل والأكمل
And (it is) disliked (prostrating) on the fold of his turban without necessity of heat, cold, or roughness of the ground. And al-kawr (means) a turn. It is said: he wound the turban and wound it – he twisted it around his head. And this turban (has) ten turns and twenty turns. Thus in al-Mughrib, and it is with fath of the kaf as Ibn Amir Hajj recorded. Al-Bukhari said in his Sahih: al-Hasan said: The people used to prostrate on the turban and the cap. And because it is a barrier that does not prevent prostration, so it is permissible, like the khuff and the sandal. And the scholar Ibn Amir Hajj gave here a good reminder: that the validity of prostration on the fold is when it is on the forehead or part of it. As for if it is on the head only, and he prostrated on it and his forehead did not touch the ground, then the prayer is not valid due to lack of prostration in its place. And many common people are careless about this and think it permissible. And the apparent (view) is that the dislike is tanzihi (non-prohibitive) because of the transmission of his (the Prophet's) action and his companions' prostration on the turban teaching permissibility, so it is not tahrimi. And Abu Dawud reported that the Messenger of Allah saw a man who had wound his turban on his forehead, so he uncovered his forehead, guiding to what is better and more perfect.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the disliked nature of prostrating on the fold (kawr) of the turban. The primary ruling states it is makruh to prostrate on the turban's fold unless there is a genuine need such as extreme heat, cold, or rough ground. The commentator then defines 'kawr' as a twist or turn of the turban, noting that turbans can have multiple folds. He cites a statement from al-Hasan al-Basri (as recorded by al-Bukhari) that the Prophet's companions used to prostrate on their turbans and caps, indicating permissibility. The author then clarifies an important point from Ibn Amir Hajj: if the turban fold is placed on the forehead (or part of it) and he prostrates, it is valid; but if the turban is only on the head and the forehead does not touch the ground, the prayer is invalid because the obligation (forehead prostration) is not fulfilled. Many common people are negligent about this. The commentator concludes that the dislike is tanzihi (non-prohibitive) because the Prophet himself prostrated on his turban to teach permissibility, and he also guided a man to uncover his forehead for the more complete practice. This shows that while it is permissible, it is better to avoid it.

Key Terms
كَوْر (kawr): A fold or twist of the turban wound around the head.
الكراهة التنزيهية (al-karahah al-tanzihiyyah): A non-prohibitive dislike; doing it is not sinful but leaving it is better.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prostrating on the turban at all
B) If the turban fold is on the head and the forehead does not touch the ground
C) If the turban has more than ten folds
D) If there is no necessity

Key Takeaway
Prostration on the turban fold is permissible but makruh tanzihi; however, the absolute condition is that the forehead must touch the ground, otherwise the prayer is invalid.

Two Disliked Acts in Prostration: Picture and Nose Omission
A commentary on two disliked acts in prostration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on prostrating on a picture and omitting the nose, and explain the underlying reasons for each ruling.

Core Idea
Prostrating on a picture or omitting the nose violates the wajib acts of prayer.

This lesson examines two specific rulings in the Hanafi fiqh of salah related to prostration (sajdah). The first deals with the object on which one prostrates, and the second concerns the parts of the body that must touch the ground. Both actions are considered disliked (makruh), but the underlying reasons differ, reflecting important principles about worship and avoidance of resemblance to non-Muslim practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts and their rulings
Read the text to see the two acts: (1) prostrating on a picture of a living being, (2) restricting prostration to the forehead only (omitting the nose). Note that the first is makruh (disliked) and the second is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The text explicitly says the dislike for omitting the nose is tahrimiyyah.
2
Understand the reasons (talil)
For the picture: because it resembles worshipping the image. For omitting the nose: because it leaves a wajib act—joining the nose to the forehead is obligatory (wajib) in sujood.
The reasoning is based on avoiding shirk-like acts and fulfilling the wajib of sujood.
3
Recognize the distinction between makruh tanzih and makruh tahrimi
Prostrating on a picture is makruh tanzih (lightly disliked), while omitting the nose without excuse is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, sinful).
Remember that makruh tahrimi is almost haram and requires sajdah al-sahw if done in prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) يكره السجود ( على صورة) - حيو ران؛ لأنه يشبه عبادتها، (وَ) يكره (الاقتصارُ على الجبهة) في السجود (بلا عـــذر بالأنف)، لترك الواجب، وهـو : ضم الأنف إليها، والكراهة: تحريمية
And (it is) disliked (prostration) upon a picture – of animals – because it resembles worshipping them. And (it is) disliked (restricting) to the forehead in prostration (without an excuse) (by the nose), due to leaving the obligation, which is: joining the nose to it. And the dislike is tahrimiyyah.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This lesson covers two separate rulings on disliked acts during prostration. First, it is makruh to prostrate on a picture of a living being, as it resembles the worship of idols or images. Second, it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) to restrict prostration to the forehead alone without placing the nose on the ground, unless there is a valid excuse. The underlying reason is that joining the nose to the forehead is wajib (obligatory) in the Hanafi school, and omitting it without excuse is a violation. The commentator explicitly states the dislike level as tahrimi for this omission.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wajib): An obligatory act (less than fard) whose omission in prayer requires sajdah al-sahw or makes the prayer makruh tahrimi.
الكراهة التحريمية (al-karahah al-tahrimiyyah): A prohibitively disliked act; it is sinful to perform but not as severe as haram.
السجود (al-sujud): Prostration, a pillar of the prayer.
صورة (surah): A picture or image of a living being.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Disliked Places for Prayer: Road, Bathroom, Graveyard, etc.
Commentary on disliked locations for prayer with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven places where prayer is disliked according to the hadith and explain the Hanafi ruling for each, including the operative cause and exceptions.

Core Idea
Prayer is disliked in seven specific places due to impurity, disruption, or satanic presence, but the ruling is karahah, not tahrim.

This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Idah presents the Hanafi position on disliked locations for prayer. Based on a hadith listing seven places, the commentator explains the reasoning behind each prohibition and clarifies exceptions, such as a designated prayer area in a graveyard. Understanding this ruling helps you apply correct fiqh in everyday situations and avoid unnecessary sin while keeping prayer valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its narrators
Locate the hadith cited by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi from Ibn Umar. Note the seven places listed and the explicit prohibition (nahy).
The hadith is ahad (single-chain) with zanni thubut, which affects the ruling level.
2
List the seven places and their illah
For each place, identify the cause for dislike mentioned in the commentary: impurity, obstruction, satanic presence, or resemblance to non-Muslims.
The commentator expands on the bathroom with two opinions: 1) impurity, 2) house of devils.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle of nahy
Recognize that the Hanafi school interprets nahy (prohibition) as karahah (dislike) rather than tahrim (prohibition) unless there is additional evidence of absolute prohibition.
This principle is based on usul: al-nahy yufidu al-karahah.
4
Note exceptions and gradations
Identify where the ruling becomes less strict: e.g., in a graveyard with a designated prayer area, or in a washed spot of the bathroom according to one opinion.
The commentator prefers the second opinion (general dislike in bathroom) due to the generality of the hadith.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the relied-upon position is to avoid these places altogether, but if necessary, prayer is permissible yet disliked.
The passage also notes that praying on top of the Ka'ba is disliked because it contradicts veneration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) تكــره (الصلاة في الطريق)؛ لأن فيه منع الناس عن المرور وشغله بما ليس له؛ لأنـهـا حــق العامة للمرور، (و) في (الحَمَّامِ)، (وَفي المَخْرَج)، أي: الكنيف، (وَفي المَقْبَرَةِ) وفي أمثالها، لما رواه ابن ماجه والترمذي عن ابن عمر ( أَنَّ رَسُولَ الله نَهَى أَنْ يُصَلَّى فِي سَبْعَةِ مَوَاطِنَ فِي الْمَرْبَلَةِ وَالْمَجْزَرَةِ وَالْمَقْبَرَةِ وَقَارِعَةِ الطَّرِيقِ وَفِي الْحَمَّامِ وَمَعَاطِنِ الإِبِلِ وَفَوْقَ ظَهْرِ بَيْتِ اللهِ ) انتهى. معــاطن الإبــل: مباركها، جمع معطن، اسم مكان من عطن يعطن كنصر ينصر، والمزبلة بفتح الميم مع فتح الباء وضمها - وهي: ملقى الزبل، أي: السرقين، والمجزرة: - بفتح الميم مع الزاي وضمها أيضاً : موضع الجزارة، أي: فعل الجزار أي القصاب، والمغتسل : بضم الميم وفتح السين- مكان الاغتسال، والعلة كونها موضع النجاسة، وألحق بها المغتسل، لأنه مصب النجاسة والأوساخ والـنـهـي عـن الصلاة في الحمام لمعنيين أحدهما أنه مصب الغسالات فعلى هذا لا يكره في سائره، فإذا غسل منه موضعاً ليس فيه تمثال لا تكره فيه، والثاني: أن الحمام بيت الشياطين، فعلى هذا تكــره في جميعه غسل موضعه أو لا، والأولى أن لا يصلي فيه إلا لضرورة كخوف فوت الوقت ونحوه لإطلاق الحديث وأما الصلاة في موضع جلوس الحمامي فقال قاضي خان»: لا بأس بها، وفي « الفتاوى » : لا بأس بالصلاة في المقبرة إذا كان فيها موضع أعدَّ للصلاة وليس فيه قبر، وهذا لأن الكراهية معللة بالتشبه بأهل الكتاب، وهو منتف فيما كان على الصفة المذكورة، والكراهة فوق الكعبة المشرفة ينافي تعظيمها، كما سنذكره )
And (it is) disliked (prayer) in the road, because it prevents people from passing and occupies it with something not its own, as it is a public right of passage. And in the bathroom, and in the toilet (al-makhraj), i.e., the privy. And in the graveyard, and in similar places, due to what Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Ibn Umar: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade praying in seven places: the dunghill (al-madbala), the slaughterhouse (al-majzara), the graveyard, the middle of the road, the bathroom, the resting places of camels (ma'atin al-ibil), and on top of the House of Allah.' End. Ma'atin al-ibil: their lying places, plural of m'atin, a place name from 'atana ya'tunu like nasara yansuru. Al-madbala (with fatha on mim and fatha or damma on ba): the place where dung is thrown, i.e., manure. Al-majzara (with fatha on mim and fatha or damma on za): the place of slaughtering, i.e., the act of the butcher. And al-mughtasal (with damma on mim and fatha on sin): the place of washing. The cause is that it is a place of impurity. And they annexed to it the washing place because it is a receptacle of impurity and filth. And the prohibition of prayer in the bathroom is for two reasons: one, it is a receptacle of washings; on this basis, it is not disliked in its other parts. So if one washes a spot in it that has no image, it is not disliked in it. The second: the bathroom is a house of devils; on this basis, it is disliked in all of it, whether its spot is washed or not. The better opinion is not to pray in it except for necessity such as fear of losing the time and the like, due to the generality of the hadith. As for prayer in the place where the bath attendant sits, Qadi Khan said: there is no harm. And in al-Fatawa: there is no harm in praying in the graveyard if there is a spot prepared for prayer with no grave in it. This is because the dislike is reasoned by resemblance to the People of the Book, and this is absent in the described situation. And the dislike (of praying) above the Ka'ba contradicts its veneration, as we will mention.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the dislike of prayer in certain locations. The primary ruling is that prayer is makruh in seven places based on a hadith narrated by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi. The commentator lists the places: the dunghill, slaughterhouse, graveyard, middle of the road, bathroom, camel resting places, and on top of the Ka'ba. He explains each: 'madbala' is a garbage dump, 'majzara' is a slaughterhouse, 'ma'atin al-ibil' are camel resting places. Then he discusses the reasons for the dislike. For the bathroom, there are two opinions: one that it is due to impurity (so it is disliked only in the impure areas, not elsewhere), and the other that it is a haunt of devils (so it is disliked everywhere). The better view is to avoid it entirely except when necessary. For the graveyard, if there is a designated prayer area without a grave, it is not disliked, as the reason (resembling People of the Book) is absent. He also notes that praying on top of the Ka'ba is disliked as it violates its veneration.

Key Terms
المَقْبَرَة (al-maqbarah): A graveyard; a place where graves are located.
المَخْرَج (al-makhraj): The toilet; a place for relieving oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible without dislike
B) Makruh because it is a house of devils
C) Haram
D) Invalid

Key Takeaway
Prayer in the seven listed places is disliked (makruh) but valid; exceptions exist for clean, designated spots like a prayer area in a graveyard.

Disliked: praying in another's land without permission
Ruling on praying on another's land without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on praying on another's land without permission and determine the preferred option when forced to choose between the road and another's land, based on the type of land and the owner's religion.

Core Idea
Praying on another's land without permission is makruh, but the preference between road and land depends on presumed consent and property rights.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on praying in someone else's property without explicit permission. While generally disliked, there are nuances when one must choose between the road and another's land. The ruling balances public rights, owner interests, and presumption of consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The passage begins with the general rule: It is makruh (disliked) to pray on another's land without permission. State this ruling clearly.
2
Examine the forced-choice scenario
When forced to choose between praying in the road or on another's land, the ruling becomes conditional. Recognize that both options have drawbacks, so a preference is given based on context.
3
Apply the cultivation condition
If the land is cultivated (e.g., farmland), it is better to pray in the road because the owner would dislike damage to crops, and the road is a public right.
4
Apply the owner's religion condition (uncultivated land)
If the land is uncultivated and belongs to a Muslim, it is better to pray on the land because the owner is likely pleased (he gains reward). If it belongs to a non-Muslim, it is better to pray in the road because the owner would not be pleased.
The road is not exclusively for the non-Muslim.
5
Conclude the preference
Based on the above conditions, determine whether to choose the road or the land. The ruling is derived from the principle of presuming consent (zahir al-rida).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) تكره الصلاة في أرض الغير بلا رضاه) وإذا ابتلي بين الصلاة في الطريق أو أرض الغير فإن كانت مزروعة فالأفضل أن يصلي في الطريق، لأن له حقاً فيه، ولا حق له في أرض الغير، وإن لم تكن مزروعة، فإن كانت لمسلم يصلي فيها، لأن الظـاهـر أنـه يـرضـى بـهـا، لأنه ينال أجراً من غير اكتساب منه، ولا إذن في الطريق؛ لأنه حق المسلم والكافر، وإن كانت الأرض لكافر يصلي في الطريق؛ لأنه لا يرضى بها كذا في البرهان» ، والطريق ليست للكافر على الخصوص
And (it is) disliked (to pray) in someone else's land without his permission. And if one is faced with the choice between praying in the road or in someone else's land: if it (the land) is cultivated, then it is better to pray in the road, because he has a right in it (the road), and he has no right in someone else's land. And if it is not cultivated, then if it belongs to a Muslim, he should pray in it, because apparently he (the owner) would be pleased with it, as he receives reward without earning (from the prayer). And there is no permission in the road because it is the right of both Muslim and non-Muslim. But if the land belongs to a non-Muslim, he should pray in the road, because he (the owner) would not be pleased with it. Thus in al-Burhan. And the road is not exclusively for the non-Muslim.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on praying on someone else's land without explicit permission. The general ruling is that it is makruh to do so. However, when one is faced with a choice between praying in the road (which is also disliked) and on someone else's land, the preference depends on the type of land. If the land is cultivated, it is better to pray in the road because the road is a public right and the landowner would not appreciate damage to crops. If the land is uncultivated and belongs to a Muslim, it is better to pray on the land because it is likely the owner would consent (as he gains reward from the prayer occurring on his property). If the land belongs to a non-Muslim, it is better to pray in the road because the non-Muslim owner is unlikely to consent. The commentator cites al-Burhan for this ruling.

Key Terms
أرض الغير (ard al-ghayr): Land belonging to someone else (not the praying person).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When the owner is a Muslim and likely to consent
C) When the land is cultivated
D) Never, because the road is a public right

Key Takeaway
Praying on another's land without permission is makruh; however, when forced to choose, prefer the road for cultivated or non-Muslim land, but prefer the land for uncultivated Muslim land due to presumed consent.

Praying While Suppressing Bodily Waste is Makruh
Commentary on the ruling of praying while suppressing bodily waste

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (makruh) for praying while suppressing urine, stool, or wind, and identify the evidence from hadith and the reasoning based on khushu'.

Core Idea
Praying while suppressing urine, stool, or wind is makruh because it prevents khushu'.

This passage explains that it is disliked to pray while actively suppressing the need to relieve oneself. The ruling applies whether the need arises before or during the prayer and is supported by a hadith that strongly discourages such a state and by the rational consideration that it distracts from the required concentration. We will explore the evidence and how this ruling is applied in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is praying while suppressing urine, stool, or wind. The ruling is makruh (disliked).
The ruling applies whether the need is felt before starting prayer or during it.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The hadith narrated by Abu Dawud: 'لا يحل لأحد يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن يصلي وهو حاقن حتى يتخفف' (It is not permissible for anyone who believes in Allah and the Last Day to pray while suppressing urine until he relieves himself.)
The hadith uses 'la yahillu' which indicates a strong prohibition.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'la yahillu' is interpreted in the Hanafi school as a strong prohibition, but the ruling is downgraded to karahah due to considerations of hardship (istihsan).
The hadith's prohibition is not absolute; it is understood in context of avoiding unnecessary hardship.
4
Identify the secondary evidence
The commentator adds that suppressing these needs distracts from khushu' (humility and concentration), which is a required quality of prayer.
This 'illa (reason) supports the ruling and shows the underlying wisdom.
5
Apply the ruling to practical cases
If a person needs to relieve himself before prayer, he should do so if time permits. If time is very short, the prayer is still valid but remains makruh.
The ruling is not that the prayer is invalid, but that it is disliked due to lack of khushu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) تكره الصلاة حال كونه (مدافعاً لأحد الأَخْبَتَيْنِ) هما: البول والغائط، (أو) في حال مدافعة ( الريح)، وسواء كان به ذلك قبل افتتاح الصلاة أو بعده؛ لأن المعنى يجمعهما كذا في ( التجنيس والمزيد » لقوله : ( لا يحل لأحدٍ يؤمن بالله واليومِ الآخِرِ أَنْ يُصَلِّي وَهُوَ حَاقِنٌ حَتَّى يَتَخَفَّفَ ) (۳) رواه أبو داود، ولأنه يشغل به عن الخشوع
And (it is) disliked to pray while he is suppressing one of the two foul things, which are urine and stool, or while suppressing wind. And it is the same whether it occurs before beginning the prayer or after, because the same meaning unites them. Thus in al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid, due to his saying: 'It is not permissible for anyone who believes in Allah and the Last Day to pray while he is suppressing (urine) until he relieves himself.' Reported by Abu Dawud. And because it distracts from khushu' (humility).
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This lesson gives the ruling that praying while actively suppressing urine, stool, or passing gas is makruh. The ruling applies whether one feels the need before starting the prayer or during it. The commentator provides two proofs: first, a hadith from Abu Dawud that prohibits praying while suppressing urine until one relieves oneself; second, the rational reason that preoccupation with these needs prevents khushu' (concentration and humility) in prayer. The text references 'al-Tajnis wa al-Mazid' as the source for the unified ruling.

Key Terms

الخشوع (al-khushu'): Humility and concentration in prayer.

Ruling on Praying with Non-Prohibitive Impurity
Commentary on ruling of praying with light impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for praying with non-prohibitive impurity, distinguish between the general dislike and the exceptions, and explain the reasoning (talil) based on the proof cited in the text.

Core Idea
Praying with small impurity is disliked, but excused if you fear missing time or congregation.

In this lesson, we examine a commentary from Imdad al Fattah on Nur al-Idah concerning a practical matter: what if you have a small amount of impurity on your clothing, body, or prayer area? The base ruling is that it is disliked (makruh), but the commentator provides important exceptions and explains the reasoning behind them. Understanding this nuance helps you apply fiqh correctly in real situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is praying while a non-prohibitive impurity is present. The default ruling according to the commentator is: it is makruh (disliked). This applies whether the impurity is on the garment, body, or place.
Non-prohibitive means the impurity is below the threshold that invalidates prayer (e.g., less than a dirham of heavy najasa).
2
Identify the exceptions to the default ruling
The text provides two exceptions: (1) if one fears the prayer time will expire, or (2) if one fears missing the congregation (jama'ah). In these cases, one should pray without removing the impurity—it is no longer disliked.
The reasoning: missing the time is haram, and congregation is a confirmed sunnah. So the dislike is overridden by the greater obligations.
3
Identify the ruling when neither exception applies
If there is no fear of missing time or congregation, the text says it is recommended (mandub) to cut the prayer, remove the impurity, and restart.
This shows that removing the impurity is superior, but not obligatory when the amount is non-prohibitive.
4
Analyze the underlying proof (talil)
The commentator gives two reasons: (a) 'due to the desirability of leaving the difference of opinion' (istihbab al-khuruj min al-khilaf) – meaning Hanafis adopt the makruh ruling to avoid the view that any impurity invalidates; (b) 'because taking the prayer out of its time is haram' and 'congregation is a confirmed sunnah' – these outweigh the dislike.
Thus the proof is a combination of istihsan based on ikhtilaf and prioritizing higher obligations.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa: It is makruh to pray with non-prohibitive impurity when one can easily remove it, but if removing it would cause missing the time or congregation, one should pray as is. In other cases, it is better to cut and remove the impurity.
This is consistent with zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) تـكـره الصلاة (مع نجاسة غير مانعة) - تقدم بيانها (۳) - وسواء كانت بثوبه، أو بدنه، أو مكانه، لاستحباب الخروج الخلاف، ( إلا إذا خاف فَوْتَ الوقتِ، أو) فوت (الجماعة)، فحينئذ يصلي وهو بتلك الحالة؛ لأن إخراج الصلاة عن وقتها حرام، ومع الجماعة سنة مؤكدة، ( وإلا ) أي : وإن لم يخف الـفـوت (نُدِبَ قطعها ) أي: قطع الصلاة وإزالة النجاسة والتخفف، والقطع للإكمال مندوب إليه، كمـا لـو أقيمت من
And (it is) disliked to pray with non-prohibitive najasa – its explanation has preceded (3) – whether it is on his garment, his body, or his place, due to the desirability of leaving the difference of opinion. Except if he fears the passing of the time or the (loss of) congregation, then he prays in that state, because taking the prayer out of its time is haram, and (praying in) congregation is a confirmed sunnah. And otherwise – i.e., if he does not fear loss – then it is recommended to cut it, i.e., to cut the prayer and remove the najasa and relieve himself. And cutting to complete (the prayer) is recommended, as if the congregation is established...
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This lesson discusses the ruling on praying while there is a non-prohibitive amount of impurity (such as a small amount of blood or urine that is below the threshold that invalidates prayer). According to the commentator, it is generally makruh to pray in such a state, whether the impurity is on the clothing, body, or ground. The reason is to avoid differing opinions. However, there is an exception: if one fears that the time for the prescribed prayer will pass, or that the congregation will be missed, then one should pray without removing the impurity. This is because missing the prayer time is haram, and congregation is a strongly recommended sunnah. If there is no such fear, it is recommended (mandub) to break the prayer, remove the impurity, and then restart. The text cuts off at the end but likely continues with an example about congregation.

Key Terms
النجاسة غير المانعة (al-najasa ghayr al-mani'ah): An impurity that is small in quantity such that it does not prevent the validity of the prayer (e.g., less than a dirham of blood).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school deems it disliked to pray with non-prohibitive impurity, but excuses it when necessary to preserve the prayer time or congregation; otherwise, it is recommended to remove the impurity before or during prayer.

Dislike of Prayer in the Bathhouse: Two 'Illal and Their Scope
Explaining two reasons for dislike of prayer in bathhouse and their scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two reasons for the dislike of prayer in a bathhouse, explain how each affects the scope of the ruling, and identify the mufta bihi and the exception for the attendant's seat.

Core Idea
Prayer in a bathhouse is disliked, but the extent depends on the ʿilla—either limited to wastewater areas or the whole building; necessity permits it.

The Hanafi school teaches that prayer in a bathhouse is makruh tanzih, but why? The scholars derived two possible reasons (ʿillal) from the texts, and these reasons lead to different scopes of dislike. This lesson unpacks those reasons, shows which one is preferred, and highlights a specific exemption for the attendant's seat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The passage begins with 'والنهي عن الصلاة في الحمام' – the prohibition of prayer in the bathhouse. What is the hukm level? It is makruh tanzih (slightly disliked). The prohibition is based on two possible reasons, not on a single decisive text.
The word 'النهي' here indicates dislike rather than absolute prohibition.
2
Extract the first ʿilla
The first reason is that the bathhouse is 'مصب الغسالات' (a place where washings flow). According to this view, the dislike is restricted only to the area where wastewater collects. If that spot is washed clean, prayer there becomes permissible.
This ʿilla focuses on physical impurity (najāsah) in the location.
3
Extract the second ʿilla
The second reason is 'أن الحمام بيت الشياطين' (the bathhouse is a house of devils). This ʿilla extends the dislike to the entire building, regardless of cleanliness, because of the spiritual impurity or presence of devils.
This ʿilla is based on a hadith quoted in other sources (e.g., Musnad Ahmad).
4
Determine the preferred view
The author states 'والأولى أن لا يصلي فيه إلا لضرورة' – it is better not to pray in it except for necessity (like fear of losing time). This indicates that the second ʿilla prevails as the operative reason (mufta bihi), making the entire bathhouse disliked.
The phrase 'لإطلاق الحديث' (due to the absolute hadith) supports this preference.
5
Note the specific exemption
Qadi Khan said there is no harm in praying in the place where the bath attendant sits. This shows that a clean, dry part of the bathhouse that is not used for washing may be an exception, adding nuance to the general ruling.
The attendant's seat is kept clean and free from both wastewater and the specific spiritual taint mentioned in the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنهي عن الصلاة في الحمام لمعنيين أحدهما أنه مصب الغسالات فعلى هذا لا يكره في سائره، فإذا غسل منه موضعاً ليس فيه تمثال لا تكره فيه، والثاني: أن الحمام بيت الشياطين، فعلى هذا تكره في جميعه غسل موضعه أو لا، والأولى أن لا يصلي فيه إلا لضرورة كخوف فوت الوقت ونحوه لإطلاق الحديث وأما الصلاة في موضع جلوس الحمامي فقال قاضي خان: لا بأس بها
And the prohibition of prayer in the bathhouse is for two reasons: one is that it is a place where washings flow, so according to this, it is not disliked in other parts; if one washes a spot in it that has no image, it is not disliked. The second is that the bathhouse is a house of devils, so according to this, it is disliked in all of it, whether one washes its spot or not. The preferred view is that one should not pray in it except for necessity, like fear of losing the time, etc., due to the absolute hadith. As for prayer in the place where the bath attendant sits, Qadi Khan said: there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that prayer in a bathhouse is disliked (makruh). The author mentions two possible reasons (ʿilla) for the ruling. The first reason—that it is a place where washings (wastewater) collect—restricts the dislike only to the area where the water flows, not the entire bathhouse. The second reason—that the bathhouse is a house of devils—extends the dislike to the entire building. The preferred view (al-ūlā) is to avoid prayer in a bathhouse altogether except for necessity, such as fear of losing the prayer time, based on a general hadith (the exact wording is not quoted but is from Sahih Muslim). Additionally, Qadi Khan specifies that the seat of the bathhouse attendant, which is kept clean, is not disliked for prayer. This shows nuance in the Hanafi position: while the general ruling is dislike, the specific location and intention matter.

Key Terms
الحمام (al-ḥammām): The bathhouse, a place with hot water and steam used for bathing, traditionally also used for ritual purification.
الغسالات (al-ghassālāt): The washings or wastewater that flows from people washing themselves.
بيت الشياطين (bayt al-shayāṭīn): House of devils, a description of the bathhouse based on hadith, implying spiritual impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a place where washings flow
B) It is a house of devils
C) It resembles a place of worship of the People of the Book
D) It is a place of impurity

Key Takeaway
The dislike of prayer in a bathhouse has two possible causes; the preferred view is to avoid it throughout the building except for necessity, with a specific exemption for the attendant's seat.

Prayer on Another's Land Without Permission
Ruling on prayer in another's land with conditional preferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying in another's land without permission and apply the preferential rules when forced to choose between the road and someone else's land.

Core Idea
Prayer on another's land without permission is disliked, but the owner's religion and cultivation status determine the better option when forced.

When you need to pray but only have access to another person's land or a public road, which is the better choice? The Hanafi school gives specific guidance based on the land's cultivation and the owner's faith. This lesson explores the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
State the default hukm: praying in another's land without permission is makruh tanzih (disliked). Recognize that this is due to potential harm to the owner's rights.
2
Understand the forced-choice scenario
If you must choose between the road and another's land, the preference depends on two conditions: is the land cultivated (mazru'ah) or not? And who owns it—Muslim or non-Muslim?
3
Apply the rules for each case
(1) Cultivated land: road is better because it is a shared right and the land is productive. (2) Uncultivated Muslim land: pray on the land because the Muslim is likely pleased. (3) Uncultivated non-Muslim land: road is better because the non-Muslim is not pleased.
The reasoning illustrates the Hanafi principle of avoiding harm and considering the owner's presumed consent.
4
Recognize the legal basis
The ruling is derived from rational reasoning (istihsan) based on ownership rights and the concept of benefit (maslahah). No explicit nas is cited, but it aligns with general Shariah principles.
This is an example of expansion (istidlal) using circumstantial evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) تكره الصلاة في أرض الغير بلا رضاه وإذا ابتلي بين الصلاة في الطريق أو أرض الغير فإن كانت مزروعة فالأفضل أن يصلي في الطريق، لأن له حقاً فيه، ولا حق له في أرض الغير، وإن لم تكن مزروعة، فإن كانت لمسلم يصلي فيها، لأن الظاهر أنه يرضى بها، لأنه ينال أجراً من غير اكتساب منه، ولا إذن في الطريق؛ لأنه حق المسلم والكافر، وإن كانت الأرض لكافر يصلي في الطريق؛ لأنه لا يرضى بها كذا في البرهان، والطريق ليست للكافر على الخصوص
And it is disliked to pray in another's land without his permission. If one is faced with the choice between praying on the road or in another's land: if the land is cultivated, it is better to pray on the road, because one has a right on the road but no right in another's land. If it is not cultivated, then if it belongs to a Muslim, he should pray on it because it is apparent that the Muslim is pleased with it, as he obtains reward without any effort from him, and there is no permission on the road because it is a right of both Muslims and disbelievers. If the land belongs to a disbeliever, he should pray on the road because the disbeliever is not pleased with it. This is as mentioned in al-Burhan.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on prayer in someone else's land without permission. The general ruling is that it is disliked due to the lack of ownership and potential harm to the owner. The text then discusses a scenario where one must choose between praying on a public road or on another's land. The preference depends on two factors: (1) whether the land is cultivated (mazrūʿah) or not, and (2) whether the owner is Muslim or non-Muslim. For cultivated land, praying on the road is better because the road is a public right and the land is productive. For uncultivated land, if the owner is Muslim, it is better to pray on the land because it is likely the Muslim owner would be pleased (since he gains spiritual reward without effort), and the road is a shared right with non-Muslims. If the owner is non-Muslim, the road is better because the non-Muslim would not be pleased. This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding harm and respecting ownership rights.

Key Terms
أرض الغير (arḍ al-ghayr): Land belonging to someone else, not owned or leased by the person praying.
الطريق (al-ṭarīq): The public road or path accessible to all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pray on the road
B) Pray on the land
C) Both are equal
D) It is not allowed to pray in either

Key Takeaway
Prayer on another's land without permission is disliked, and when forced to choose, the better option depends on whether the land is cultivated and the owner's faith.

Prayer Near Impurity: Dislike, Not Invalidity
Ruling statement with brief causal explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prayer performed near an impurity and explain the legal reasoning (ʿilla) behind its dislike.

Core Idea
Proximity to impurity does not invalidate prayer but is disliked.

This lesson addresses a common practical scenario: a person prays while an impurity is nearby but not on their body or clothes. The Hanafi school rules that such prayer is valid but disliked. Understanding the distinction between invalidity and dislike is essential for correctly assessing one's prayer. The reasoning—that proximity may be treated like contact—affects how we evaluate potential contamination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? (Prayer) What is the hukm? (Makruh tanzih – disliked, not forbidden or invalid).
2
Understand the ʿilla (legal reason)
The text says 'because something close to a thing may be given its ruling.' This is the analogy: proximity to impurity is treated similarly to direct contact for the purpose of dislike, but not for invalidity.
3
Distinguish between dislike and invalidity
Confirm that the prayer is still valid (sahih) even though disliked. Invalidity would require actual contact with impurity.
4
Connect to the broader command
The command to avoid impurity (Quran 74:4, hadith) extends to the place of prayer. Being near impurity without necessity contradicts proper reverence.
This is a matter of adab (etiquette) rather than a strict condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) تكره الصلاة إذا فعلها (قريباً من نجاسة)، لأن ما قرب من الشيء قد يعطى حكمه، وقد أمرنا بتجنب النجاسة ومكانها
And it is disliked to pray when one is near impurity, because something close to a thing may be given its ruling, and we have been commanded to avoid impurity and its place.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This is a brief ruling that prayer is disliked if performed in close proximity to an impure substance. The reason (ʿilla) given is that proximity to impurity brings the same ruling as being in contact with it. The command to avoid impurity (from the Quran and Sunnah) extends to the place where impurity is present, so being near it without a barrier is considered similar to being in it. This ruling is part of the Hanafi emphasis on avoiding anything that may compromise the purity of the prayer place.

Key Terms
قريباً من نجاسة (qarīban min najāsah): Being close to an impurity, such that it could affect the prayer spot.
كراهية (karāhiyah): Dislike (makruh tanzih); a ruling that indicates an act is discouraged but not sinful or invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because proximity to impurity is treated like contact, and we are commanded to avoid impurity and its place.
C) Because the impurity spreads through the air.
D) Because the Prophet ﷺ strictly forbade praying near any impurity.

Key Takeaway
Prayer performed near an impurity is valid but disliked due to the principle that proximity may share the ruling of contact and the general command to avoid impurity.

Dislike of Prayer with Non-Preventing Impurity and Exception for Fear of Missing Time
Ruling on prayer with minor impurity, with exception when missing time or congregation is feared

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on praying with non-preventing impurity and explain when the exception applies.

Core Idea
Prayer with non-preventing impurity is disliked, but permissible if time or congregation is at risk.

In this passage, the author addresses the ruling on praying while having a minor impurity that does not invalidate the prayer. While it is generally disliked (makruh tanzih), an exception is made when one fears losing the prayer time or the congregational prayer. This exception prioritizes greater obligations over the recommendation to be free from impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Recognize that praying with non-preventing impurity is makruh tanzih (disliked) but valid.
The impurity does not invalidate the prayer due to its small quantity.
2
Determine the exception condition
Note the two conditions that override the dislike: fear of losing the prayer time or fear of missing the congregation.
Both are considered stronger obligations/recommendations.
3
Understand the rationale
Delaying a fard prayer beyond its time is haram; congregational prayer is sunnah mu'akkadah. Hence, avoiding a greater harm or loss takes priority.
This illustrates the Hanafi principle of prioritizing obligations.
4
Apply the ruling in absence of fear
If no fear of losing time or congregation, it is recommended (mandub) to cut the prayer, remove the impurity, and repeat.
Cutting is for completion, not invalidation.
5
Distinguish from preventing impurity
If the impurity exceeds the threshold (e.g., more than a dirham of blood), it prevents validity and must be removed before prayer.
This lesson only covers non-preventing amounts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) تـكـره الصلاة (مع نجاسة غير مانعة) - تقدم بيانها (۳) - وسواء كانت بثوبه، أو بدنه، أو مكانه، لاستحباب الخروج الخلاف، ( إلا إذا خاف فَوْتَ الوقتِ، أو) فوت (الجماعة)، فحينئذ يصلي وهو بتلك الحالة؛ لأن إخراج الصلاة عن وقتها حرام، ومع الجماعة سنة مؤكدة، ( وإلا ) أي : وإن لم يخف الـفـوت (نُدِبَ قطعها ) أي: قطع الصلاة وإزالة النجاسة والتخفف، والقطع للإكمال مندوب إليه، كمـا لـو أقيمت الجماعة بعدما شرع منفرداً، وكهدم المسجد ليبنى أحسن مما كان كذا في البرهان انتهى. وقضية قوله : ( لا يحل » توجب القطع
And it is disliked to pray with non-preventing impurity—its explanation has preceded—whether it be on his clothes, his body, or his place, due to the recommendation of leaving disagreement. Except if he fears the loss of the time or the loss of the congregation, then he prays in that state, because taking the prayer out of its time is forbidden, and the congregation is a confirmed sunnah. Otherwise, i.e., if he does not fear the loss, it is recommended to cut the prayer, i.e., to cut the prayer and remove the impurity and relieve himself, and cutting the prayer for completion is recommended, just as if the congregation is established after he had started praying alone, and like demolishing a mosque to build a better one. This is as stated in al-Burhan. And the implication of his saying 'it is not permissible' necessitates cutting.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This ruling addresses prayer while having a non-preventing impurity (نجاسة غير مانعة), which is an impurity that does not prevent the validity of prayer because it is minor (e.g., less than a dirham of blood or urine). The Hanafi school considers it disliked to pray with such impurity because it is better to avoid all impurity when possible. However, there is an exception: if one fears that the prayer time will end or that the congregation will be missed, then one may pray in that state because delaying prayer beyond its time is haram (for fard prayers) and the congregation is a highly recommended sunnah. In such cases, the dislike is overridden. If there is no such fear, it is recommended (mandūb) to cut the prayer, remove the impurity, and then repeat the prayer. The text compares this to cutting a prayer if the congregation starts after one begins alone, or demolishing a mosque to rebuild it better. The principle: if a stronger obligation or recommendation conflicts, it takes priority.

Key Terms
نجاسة غير مانعة (najāsah ghayr māni'ah): Impurity that does not prevent the validity of prayer, such as less than a dirham of heavy impurity or less than a quarter of a garment of light impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage
